First Proof: Will Can Relate to Itself
First proof that will is distinct from appetite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that will (al-irāda) is distinct from appetite (al-shahwa) by showing that will can relate to itself while appetite cannot.

Core Idea
Will can will itself; appetite cannot crave craving.

Al-Jurjani opens his proof that will and appetite are distinct attributes with a simple observation: will can relate to itself—we can will to will—but appetite cannot relate to itself; it only relates to pleasures. This stark contrast reveals a fundamental difference in their natures, yet the proof's force depends on how we define will. Al-Jurjani illustrates this with the example of a sick person who says 'I crave that I crave,' which is really a metaphor for willing to crave. The subsequent commentary unpacks the implications and defends the proof against potential objections, making this a rich and nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Source Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow of the proof and commentary.
The passage blends the matn (core text) with the sharh (commentary); focus first on the core claim: will relates to itself, appetite does not.
2
Identify the Core Distinction
Notice that the proof rests on the ability of will to relate to itself (tā'alluq bi-nafsihā) versus appetite's inability to do so. Appetite only relates to pleasures (al-ladhdhāt).
3
Analyze the Sick Person Example
Consider why the sick person's statement 'I crave that I crave' is a metaphor for will. The object is mere craving, not a specific pleasure, showing that craving cannot crave itself literally.
The commentary explains that this usage is not wishful thinking (tamannī) because the craving is possible; it is a metaphor for will.
4
Examine the Critical Point on Definitions
Understand that the proof's validity depends on the definition of will. Under the definition 'belief in benefit' or 'inclination following it,' will can relate to itself. Under the 'specifying attribute' definition, it cannot.
Al-Jurjani notes that if will is defined as a specifying attribute (ṣifat mukhaṣṣiṣah) for one of two possibilities, then will cannot relate to itself because our will is not within our power.
5
Review the Commentary's Clarifications
Read the commentary's explanation of 'different in existence' and 'yearning of the soul' to see additional support for the distinction. Note how the proof does not require that all wills relate to themselves.
The commentary also defends against a possible objection: the proof does not fail if some wills do not relate to themselves, because the nature of will allows it, unlike appetite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد الرابع : الإرادة مغايرة للشهوة
الأول : الإرادة قد تتعلق بنفسها دون الشهوة فإنها لا تتعلق بنفسها بل باللذات، وإذا ذكرت متعلقة بنفسها كانت مجازاً عن الإرادة، كما قيل لمريض : ما تشتهي ؟ فقال : أشتهي أن أشتهي أي أريد أن أشتهي ( وفيه ) أي في هذا الفرق (نظر تعرفه ) أنت ( مما اخترناه ) في الإرادة ) من التعريف ) يعني أنه إذا فسر الإرادة باعتقاد النفع أو الميل التابع له، جاز تعلقها بنفسها لجواز أن يعتقد الشخص أن في اعتقاده لمنفعة فعل من الأفعال أو في ميله إليه نفعاً له ثم يميل إلى ذلك الاعتقاد وما يتبعه، وأما إذا فسرت بما اختاره من أنها صفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع فلا يجوز تعلقها بنفسها
قوله : ( مغايرة للشهوة) أي في الوجود كما يرشد إليه الدليل وصرح به الشارح. قوله : (توقان النفس إلخ) أي اشتياقها إلى المستلذات الحسية، وفيه ظهر وجه آخر للمغايرة، فإن الاشتياق لا يقارن وجودياً ليشتاق بخلاف الإرادة .
قوله : ( الأول إلخ) حاصله : أن الإرادة صفة من شأنها أن تتعلق بنفسها والشهوة صفة ليست من شأنها ذلك، فالإرادة غير الشهوة فلا يرد أن هذا الدليل لا ينفي كون الشهوة أخص من الإرادة لجواز أن يكون إرادة لا تتعلق بنفسها بناء على أن الإرادة قد تتعلق بنفسها وقد لا تتعلق . قوله (دون الشهوة) بناء على أنها ليست من المستلذات الحسية، وفيه أن الشهوة المخصوصة من الوجدانيات .
قوله : ( فإنها لا تتعلق إلخ ) إعادة للدعوى بعبارة أخرى بتنبيه بداهتها .
قوله : ( فقال : أشتهي أن أشتهي فإن مطلوبه مجرد الاشتهاء لا اشتهاء شيء معين، ولذا نزل الفعل المتعدي منزلة اللازم، فلو كان الاشتهاء بمعناه الحقيقي لزم جود الاشتهاء عند عدم الاشتهاء، لأن المشتهى لا يكون موجوداً عند حال الاشتهاء، وليس مجازاً عند التمني لكون اشتهاء المريض أمراً ممكن الوقوع غير مستبعد فهو مجاز عن الإرادة إذ لا رابع.
The third type - fourth objective: Will is different from appetite. First: Will can relate to itself, unlike appetite, for appetite does not relate to itself but to pleasures. And if it is mentioned as relating to itself, it is a metaphor for will. As it was said to a sick person: 'What do you crave?' He said: 'I crave that I crave,' meaning I will to crave. And in this distinction there is a critical point that you know from our chosen definition of will. That is, if will is interpreted as belief in benefit or the inclination following it, then its relation to itself is permissible because it is possible that a person believes that in his belief in the benefit of an action or in his inclination towards it there is benefit for him, then he inclines towards that belief and what follows it. But if it is interpreted as what he chose, that it is a specifying attribute for one of the two possibilities of the possible to occur, then its relation to itself is not permissible. His statement: 'different from appetite' means in existence, as the proof indicates and the commentator stated explicitly. His statement: 'yearning of the soul etc.' means its longing for sensory pleasures, and in it another aspect of difference appears: longing does not accompany existence for it to long, unlike will. His statement: 'First etc.' Summary: Will is an attribute whose nature is to relate to itself, and appetite is an attribute whose nature is not that. So will is not appetite. This proof does not negate that appetite is more specific than will because it is possible that there is a will that does not relate to itself, based on the fact that will may relate to itself and may not. His statement: 'without appetite' based on the fact that it is not among sensory pleasures, though appetite specifically is among innate sensations. His statement: 'for it does not relate etc.' is a restatement of the claim in other words to indicate its obviousness. His statement: 'he said: I crave that I crave' because his object is mere craving, not craving for something specific; hence the transitive verb is treated as intransitive. For if craving were in its literal sense, then existence of craving would be necessary when there is no craving, because the craved object does not exist at the moment of craving. It is not a metaphor for wishful thinking because the patient's craving is a possible occurrence, not improbable; it is a metaphor for will, as there is no fourth.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that will (al-irādah) is distinct from appetite (al-shahwah). The proof is that will can relate to itself (the will to will), while appetite cannot relate to itself—it only relates to pleasures. The author illustrates this with the example of a sick person who, when asked what he craves, replies 'I crave that I crave,' which means 'I will to crave.' The author notes that this proof depends on how will is defined. If will is defined as belief in benefit (iʿtiqād al-nafʿ) or the inclination (al-mayl) following it, then it can relate to itself because one can believe that believing in a benefit or inclining towards a benefit itself is beneficial, leading to a second-order will. But if will is defined as a specifying attribute (ṣifat mukhaṣṣiṣah) for one of two possible outcomes, then it cannot relate to itself because our will is not within our power. The commentary then explains each part: 'different from appetite' meaning distinct in existence; 'yearning of the soul' meaning longing for sensory pleasures, and this also shows difference because longing does not require existence of its object, unlike will. The commentary further clarifies that the first argument does not fail even if some wills do not relate to themselves, because the proof is based on the nature of will and appetite. The explanation of the sick person's statement shows that 'craving that I crave' is a metaphor for will because the object is mere craving, not a specific thing, and it is not wishful thinking because the craving is possible.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الشهوة
تعلق بنفسها
اعتقاد النفع
الميل
صفة مخصصة
المقدور
توقان النفس
الاشتهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinction between will and appetite is merely semantic or a matter of definition.
Correction: The distinction is based on real natures: will can relate to itself (as shown by the example), while appetite inherently cannot. The dependence on definition does not make it merely semantic; it shows that the correct definition must capture the reality.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that will is distinct from appetite?
A) Will is created, appetite is not.
B) Will can relate to itself, appetite cannot.
C) Will is always good, appetite is always evil.
D) Will is a mental act, appetite is physical.

Key Takeaway
The first proof that will is distinct from appetite is that will can relate to itself, as shown by the example of willing to will, whereas appetite cannot relate to itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have no appetite for prayer, have you ever willed to want to pray? That act of will willing itself shows your will is not bound by your appetite. How often do you engage your will in this way? Commit to one specific situation today where you will will yourself to desire what is good.
?
The sick person craved craving itself, revealing that true will is deeper than mere desire. What are you truly willing at the core of your being, independent of your appetites? Are you willing to change what you will? Ask yourself: 'What do I will that I will?' and let that shape your next decision.
?
If will is distinct from appetite, then you cannot excuse your actions by saying 'I just felt like it.' Your will is free to choose beyond your cravings. Which appetite have you been following that you should instead govern with your will? Name it now and decide to will against it.

Defending the Definition of Will: The Qayd al-Haythiyyah Response
Al-Jurjani discusses objection and defense of specifying-attribute definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the specifying-attribute definition of will by introducing the qualification of respect (qayd al-haythiyyah) to counter objections that the definition is too narrow or problematic.

Core Idea
The definition of will is saved by the implicit qualification 'inasmuch as it is such'.

This passage presents a critical objection to the common definition of will as a specifying attribute of what is within power. The objector argues that if will can relate to non-volitional things like life and death, then the restriction to maqdūr is either pointless or too narrow. Al-Jurjani responds by clarifying that the definition implicitly includes a qualification: will is a specifying attribute inasmuch as it is such, preventing it from relating to non-volitional objects. This lesson unpacks the objection and its refined response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection to the definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector challenges the specifying-attribute definition by pointing out that will could relate to non-volitional things, making the restriction to 'what is within power' superfluous.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فبطل ما قيل' which signals a refutation of a previous claim.
2
Identify the three sub-objections
The passage lists three specific problems: (1) the definition becomes a definition by the more specific, (2) will would then apply to natural acts, making it independent of knowledge, and (3) wishful thinking (tamannī) would become a type of will. Trace each one in the text.
Note that the objection skips a 'thirdly', going from first to second to fourth. This is likely a scribal anomaly.
3
Understand the response with qayd al-haythiyyah
The response is concise: the definition implicitly includes the qualification 'min haythu hiya kathālik' (inasmuch as it is such). This restricts the attribute to volitional objects only, thus defusing the objections.
This qualification is a common technique in kalam to refine definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فلا يجوز تعلقها بنفسها وكذا تعلقها بالشهوة لأنها أيضاً غير مقدورة لنا، فبطل مر من أنه إذا ذكرت متعلقة بنفسها كانت مجازاً عن الإرادة. قال في شرح المقاصد : التفسير المذكور لا يقتضي كونه متعلقاً مقدوراً، لجواز أن يكون صفة يتعلق بالمقدور وغيره ويكون من شأنها الترجيح لأحد طرفي المقدور، وإذا جاز إرادة الحياة والموت فبطل ما قيل : إن متعلق الإرادة على هذا التفسير لا يكون مقدوراً، وصح ما قيل في الفرق من أن الإرادة تتعلق بالإرادة دون الشهوة، وفيه بحث. أما أولاً : فلأنه إذا جاز تعلقها بغير المقدور يكون من شأنها الترجح بغير المقدور أيضاً، فيكون أخذ المقدور في تعريفها لغواً؛ بل مخلاً، لأنه يلزم التعريف بالأخص . نعم لو كان هذا بيان حكم من أحكامها يصح ذلك. وأما ثانياً : فلانه يلزم أن يكون هذا الشخص بالنسبة إلى الأفعال الطبيعية مريداً، فلا تكون الإرادة تابعة للعلم وأما رابعاً : فلانه يلزم كون التمني نوعا من الإرادة
فيبطل ما قيل : إن متعلق الإرادة على هذا التفسير لا يكون إلا مقدوراً فيمتنع تعلقها بالإرادة، وصح ما قيل في الفرق ويمكن أن يجاب بأن قيد الحيثية هو المتبادر من التعريف أي أنها صفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور من حيث أنها كذلك، وحينئذ لا يتعلق بغير المقدور، وأما تعلقها بالحياة والموت فممنوع
His statement: 'So it is not permissible for it to relate to itself, nor to appetite because it also is not within our power.' Then what was previously mentioned—that if it is mentioned as relating to itself it is a metaphor for will—is invalidated. He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: The mentioned interpretation does not require that its object be something within power, because it is permissible that it be an attribute that relates to what is within power and other than it, and its nature is to give preponderance to one of the two sides of what is within power. And if willing life and death is permissible, then it invalidates what was said: that the object of will according to this interpretation is only what is within power. And what was said about the difference—that will relates to will but not to appetite—is correct, but there is discussion in it. Firstly, because if it is permissible for it to relate to other than what is within power, then it becomes its nature to give preponderance also to other than what is within power, so taking 'what is within power' in its definition becomes pointless, rather defective, because it would be a definition by the more specific. Yes, if this were a statement of one of its rulings, it would be correct. Secondly, because it would necessarily follow that this person is a willer with respect to natural acts, so will would not be consequent upon knowledge. Fourthly, because it would necessarily follow that wishful thinking (tamannī) is a type of will. Then it is invalidated what was said: that the object of will according to this interpretation is only what is within power, so its relation to will is impossible. And what was said about the difference is correct. And it can be answered by saying that the qualification of respect (qayd al-haythiyyah) is the apparent meaning from the definition, i.e., it is a specifying attribute for one of the two sides of what is within power inasmuch as it is such, and then it does not relate to other than what is within power. As for its relation to life and death, it is denied.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the specifying-attribute definition of will. The objection argues that if will can relate to non-volitional things (like life and death), then including 'maqdur' (what is within power) in the definition is superfluous or misleading, since it would be a definition by the more specific. Additionally, it would imply that we will natural actions, making will not dependent on knowledge, and that wishful thinking (tamannī) would be a type of will, which is incorrect. The response is that the definition includes an implicit qualification (qayd al-haythiyyah): will is a specifying attribute for one of the two sides of what is within power inasmuch as it is such (min haythu hiya kathālik). This restriction prevents it from relating to non-volitional things. The claim that will relates to life and death is denied. Thus the original distinction stands: will can relate to will but not to appetite.

Key Terms
المقدور
قيد الحيثية
صفة مخصصة
التمني
تعريف بالأخص
شرح المقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specifying-attribute definition of will requires will to only relate to volitional objects, and any relation to non-volitional objects would invalidate the definition.
Correction: The definition includes an implicit qualification—'inasmuch as it is such'—which allows it to be a specifying attribute for volitional objects without excluding potential relations to non-volitional objects in other respects.

Checkpoint
How does al-Jurjani respond to the objection that the specifying-attribute definition of will is too narrow?
A) By denying that will can relate to non-volitional things.
B) By adding the qualification 'inasmuch as it is such' (qayd al-haythiyyah).
C) By changing the definition to something else entirely.
D) By accepting the objection and redefining will as unrelated to volition.

Key Takeaway
The specifying-attribute definition of will is defensible once we understand that it includes the implicit qualification 'inasmuch as it is such', which restricts it to volitional objects without claiming exclusivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a definition of a divine attribute that seems too restrictive or problematic, do you impatiently reject it, or do you pause to consider if there might be an implicit qualification that resolves the issue? What cost might come from rushing to judgment?
?
The objection suggested that if will could relate to non-volitional things, we would be responsible for involuntary acts like breathing. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse things that are within your power with things that are not, leading to unnecessary guilt or blame? How does clarifying the object of will help you live with more peace?
?
If the qualification 'inasmuch as it is such' is inherent in the definition of will, what does that imply about how carefully we must examine the words we use about Allah? Are there other attributes where you might be missing an implicit condition? Will you commit to studying them with the same precision?

Grammatical Analysis of Pronoun Referents in the Proof
Commentator's clarification of pronoun references in al-Ash'ari's initial conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the pronoun references in the phrase 'إذ لو كانت' and why this resolution is important for the logical flow of the argument.

Core Idea
Pronoun clarity in kalam proofs removes unnecessary logical gaps.

This passage looks like a dry grammatical note, but it actually plays a key role in ensuring the proof's logical continuity. The commentator carefully assigns the pronouns in a crucial conditional statement to avoid needing an implicit 'disjunction' that would weaken the argument. By following his analysis, you will see how even small textual clarifications can prevent misunderstandings in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms for pronouns and grammatical constructions.
The text is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the pronouns and their referents
The commentator explicitly states that the concealed pronoun in 'kanat' refers to 'al-irada' (the will) and the pronoun 'ghayraha' refers to 'al-karaha' (aversion). Verify this in the text.
3
Understand the logical consequence
This assignment avoids the need to supply an implicit disjunction (idrab). Ask yourself: Why would an implicit disjunction be problematic? How does this assignment resolve it?
The commentary explains that without this assignment, the later phrase would not follow logically.
4
Examine the need for a connected exception
The commentator notes that the author's later statement does not follow directly from the earlier one, requiring a connected exception (istidrak). See how this is a separate issue from the pronoun assignment.
5
Reflect on the grammatical anomaly
The masculine form 'al-mukhalif' is used despite referring to the feminine 'irada'. The commentator explains this as a clue for the referent. Consider why this is noteworthy.
This shows the depth of grammatical analysis in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إذ لو كانت) أي الإرادة غيرها أي تلك الكراهة رأي الشارح المسبوق فالكلام يمينه الإرادة الكراهة بجعل الضمير المستتر في كانت ،للإرادة وضمير غيرها للكراهة، فاحتاج إلى تقدير الإضراب بقوله : بل يجامع منها ضد الآخر لأن قول المصنف : لكن ضد إرادة الشيء إرادة الضد، لا يترتب على قوله : فتجامع الإرادة والكراهة، واحتاج إلى تقدير استدراك أعين. قوله: (أي أمر لا يماثلها أشار بالتفسير مع ظهور المراد إلى وجه تذكير المخالف رجوع ضميره إلى الإرادة .
His saying: 'because if it were' (idh law kanat) – meaning the will is other than it, i.e., other than that aversion, according to the view of the previous commentator, so the speech is that the concealed pronoun in 'kanat' is for the will, and the pronoun 'ghayraha' is for the aversion. Thus he needed to supply the disjunction (idrab) by his saying: 'rather it coexists with the opposite of the other' because the author's saying: 'but the opposite of willing the thing is willing the opposite' does not follow from his saying: 'so the will and aversion coexist' and he needed to supply a connected exception. His saying: 'i.e., something that does not resemble it' – he indicated by the explanation, despite the clarity of the intended meaning, the reason for the masculine form of 'al-mukhalif' referring back to the will.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and interpretive analysis of the phrase 'إذ لو كانت' from the initial proof. The commentator discusses the referents of the pronouns. He explains that the concealed pronoun in 'كانت' (kanat) refers to 'al-irada' (the will) and the pronoun 'غيرها' (ghayraha) refers to 'al-karaha' (aversion). This reading helps avoid the need to supply an implicit disjunction (idrab) in the following text. The commentator also notes that the author's later phrase 'لكن ضد إرادة الشيء إرادة الضد' does not follow directly from the earlier phrase 'فتجامع الإرادة والكراهة', requiring a connected exception (istidrak). Finally, he remarks on the masculine form of 'المخالف' (al-mukhalif) as an indicator that it refers back to the will (feminine), explaining the grammatical anomaly.

Key Terms
ضمير (damir)
مستتر (mustatir)
إضراب (idrab)
استدراك (istidrak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Grammatical details are irrelevant to theological arguments.
Correction: Grammatical analysis can prevent logical gaps and unnecessary suppositions, strengthening the argument by clarifying the intended logical connections.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the pronoun in 'kanat' (كانت) refer to?
A) The will (irada)
B) The aversion (karaha)
C) The thing itself
D) The opposite (didd)

Key Takeaway
Clarifying pronoun referents in a proof can resolve apparent logical gaps and remove the need for extraneous interpretive moves.

Refuting the Identity of Willing and Hating the Opposite
Refutation of identity claim by condition of awareness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why willing something is not identical to hating its opposite, based on the condition of awareness.

Core Idea
Awareness of the opposite is required for hatred, but will can occur without awareness.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a claim that willing a thing is the same as hating its opposite. He presents a decisive counterargument: hatred of the opposite requires awareness, but one can will something while being oblivious to its opposite. Thus, the two cannot be identical. Let's examine how this refutation unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: the commentator first acknowledges the opponent's proof, then presents his refutation focusing on the condition of awareness.
2
Identify the key condition
The commentator states that hatred of the opposite requires awareness (shuʿūr). Find where he mentions this and understand why it is crucial.
3
Understand the counterexample
He gives an example: one may will something while being heedless of its opposite. This shows that will and hatred can separate, so they are not identical.
4
Follow the summary argument
He distinguishes between self-entailment (which needs no condition) and entailment of willing for hating (which requires awareness). This clinches the refutation.
5
Consider and dismiss a nuance
Some claim the original argument only held when aware, but the commentator dismisses this as unworthy. This shows the strength of his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما ذكرتم من الدليل وإن دل بظاهره على ما ادعيتم فعندنا ما ينفيه وهو أن شرط كراهة الضد الشعورية اتفاقا وضرورة، وقد لا يشعر به حال إرادة الشيء، إذ يجوز أن يخطر شيء بالبال وتتعلق به الإرادة مع الغفلة عن ضده فتنفك حينئذ الإرادة المتعلقة بالشيء عن كراهة الضد، فلا تكون الإرادة نفسها. وبالجملة فاستلزام الشيء لنفسه لا يتوقف على شرط وهو ظاهر، واستلزام إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده متوقف على الشعور بالضد الذي ربما لا يكون حاصلاً مع حصول الإرادة، فلا تكون الإرادة نفس تلك الكراهة. ومنهم من قال: إن الشيخ لم يدع أن الإرادة عين الكراهة على الإطلاق، بل ادعى أن إرادة الشيء عين كراهة ضده حال الشعور بالضد. ولا يذهب عليك أن مثل هذا الكلام مما لا يلتفت إليه. وإذا ظهر التغاير بين إرادة الشيء وكراهة ضده بما بيناه.
Then what you mentioned of the proof, although it indicates according to its apparent meaning what you claimed, we have something that negates it: that the condition for hatred of the opposite is awareness (shuʿūr), by agreement and necessity. And one may not be aware of it at the time of willing the thing, for it is possible that something occurs to the mind and will attaches to it while being heedless of its opposite, so the will (irāda) attached to the thing separates from the hatred (karāha) of the opposite at that time, so the will is not itself [the hatred]. In summary, the self-entailment of a thing does not depend on a condition, and this is evident, but the entailment of willing a thing for hating its opposite depends on awareness of the opposite, which perhaps is not present along with the occurrence of the will, so the will is not itself that hatred. And some have said: The Shaykh did not claim that the will is identical to hatred absolutely, but rather claimed that willing a thing is identical to hating its opposite at the time of being aware of the opposite. And it should not escape you that such talk is not to be paid attention to. And when the distinction (taghāyur) between willing a thing and hating its opposite has become clear by what we have explained.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that willing a thing is identical to hating its opposite. The commentator presents an argument based on the condition of awareness. He states that the opponent's proof, even if it appears to support their claim, is refuted by the fact that hatred of the opposite requires awareness of that opposite by consensus and necessity. However, one may will something without being aware of its opposite, because a thought may occur and one's will attaches to it while being heedless of the opposite. In that case, the will separates from the hatred, so the will is not the same as the hatred. The commentator then generalizes: self-entailment does not depend on a condition, but the entailment of willing a thing for hating its opposite depends on awareness, which may be absent. Therefore, the will is not identical to that hatred. Some have suggested that the original argument only claimed identity when awareness is present, but the commentator dismisses this as unworthy of attention. Finally, he concludes that the distinction between willing and hating has been clearly shown.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
كراهة (karāha)
ضد (ḍidd)
شعور (shuʿūr)
تغاير (taghāyur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that willing a thing necessarily entails hating its opposite, so they might be identical.
Correction: The commentator shows that hatred requires awareness, but will can occur without awareness, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what condition must be present for a person to hate the opposite of what they will?
A) The will must be strong.
B) The person must be aware of the opposite.
C) The opposite must be incompatible.
D) The will must be conscious.

Key Takeaway
Willing something is not identical to hating its opposite because hatred requires awareness, which can be absent when the will is present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you pursued a goal without considering any alternative or opposite. Did you still feel hatred toward the opposite? What does that reveal about the independence of your will from your aversions?
?
The author argues that hatred requires awareness. Examine a strong dislike you hold. Is your dislike rooted in a clear awareness of the opposite, or does it linger even without that awareness? How might this lesson help you purify your intentions?
?
A refined position is dismissed as unworthy of attention. Are there beliefs you cling to that only hold under certain conditions? Are you willing to accept the full truth even when it is uncomfortable?

Does Willing Entail Hating Its Opposite?
A kalam discussion on whether willing something necessitates hating its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam debate on whether willing something necessarily entails hating its opposite, and why the author argues it does not.

Core Idea
Willing does not always entail hating its opposite; one can will both opposites from different aspects.

This passage addresses a classic problem in Islamic theology: if you will something, must you hate its opposite? The issue is important for understanding divine attributes and human volition. The author presents the positions of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali, then offers his own view, supported by reasoning about the possibility of willing both opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: question, qualification, positions, argument, counter-argument.
The text is dense; read slowly and refer to key terms.
2
Identify the main positions
Find the view of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali (that willing entails hating the opposite when aware) and the author's apparent view (that it does not, because one can will both opposites).
The author's view is signaled by 'والظاهر عند المصنف خلافه'.
3
Understand the argument about willing both opposites
The author's key argument: a person can will both opposites from one aspect, either equally or with preference, so both are willed without hatred. This shows that willingness does not necessarily entail hatred.
The commentator elaborates on this point.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for theology: if God wills something, does He hate its opposite? The author suggests it is possible to will both opposites from different aspects, which has implications for divine attributes.

متعلق الإرادة لا بد أن يكون مقارناً لها، فيلزم من إرادة الضدين اجتماعهما كان كلاماً على السند، انتهى . أقول : المراد أنه أن يريد الشخص الضدين من وجه من غير كراهة شيء منها بأن يكون وقوع كل واحد منهما منفعة مع عدم المضرة، فحينئذ يتحقق إرادة الضدين من غير كراهتهما بوجه فبطل الحكم بالاستلزام . منع قوله: (وتستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً ) قال في شرح المقاصد : لو صح هذا لكان كراهة الشيء مستلزمة لإرادة ضده المشعور به فيلزم من إرادة الشيء الذي له ضدان أن يكون كل منهما مكروهاً لكونه ضد ،المراد ومراداً لكونه ضد المكروه ولا محيص إلا بتجويزه عند تغاير الجهتين، أو تخصيص الدعوى بماله ضد واحد إلى هاهنا كلامه الضدين بخلاف إرادتهما عند الأشاعرة . قوله : ( لجواز أن يريد (الضدين وأيضاً يجوز أن لا يتعلق بالضد إرادة ولا كراهة ككثير من الأمور المشعور بها، واعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف من جواز إرادة الضدين لا يصح في معرض إبطال حكم القاضي والغزالي بالاستلزام المذكور لجواز أن يكون كل منهما مكروهاً أيضاً بجهة وإنما يصح في معرض الجواب عما لهما من الدليل الذي لم يذكره المصنف، وهو أنه لو لم يكن ضد المراد المشعور به مكروهاً لكان مراداً فيلزم إرادة الضدين وهو محال، لأن الإرادتين المتعلقتين بالضدين متضادتان فتأمل.
The third type - the sixth objective: Willing a thing entails hating its opposite. So is will entailed with hatred of the opposite? Not absolutely, for it has been shown that it is separated from hatred in some cases. Rather, with the condition of being aware of it, i.e., the opposite, there is disagreement. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Ghazali said: It is entailed, i.e., willing a thing with awareness of its opposite entails that the opposite is hated by that willer. The apparent view of the author is contrary to this, because it is possible that a person wills both opposites, each one of them, from one aspect with equal will, or one outweighs the other due to a preponderant benefit over the benefit of the other, so they are both willed but not equally. This is apparent. His statement: 'It entails that the opposite is hated' — for if it were not hated, it could be willed, so the possibility of willing both opposites would follow. Thus what was said is refuted, that it is possible that neither hate nor will attaches to the opposite concerning many perceptible things. His statement: 'Because it is possible, etc.' In Sharh al-Maqasid: This does not invalidate the ruling of al-Qadi on entailment, nor does it prove its contrary, because if it is possible to will both opposites from one aspect, it is possible to hate each of them from one aspect. Rather, it is suitable as a response to their aforementioned proof by denying the impossibility of willing both opposites because it is possible for a person to will both opposites, etc. Even if it were answered as the commentator answered that the object of will must be simultaneous with it, so willing both opposites would entail their conjunction — that would be speech on the chain. End. I say: The intended meaning is that a person can will both opposites from one aspect without hating any of them, such that the occurrence of each one is beneficial without harm. Then the willing of both opposites is realized without any hatred of them, so the ruling of entailment is invalidated. His statement: 'And it entails that the opposite is hated' — he said in Sharh al-Maqasid: If this were correct, then hating a thing would entail willing its opposite that is perceived, so from willing a thing that has two opposites, each of them would be hated for being opposite to the willed, and willed for being opposite to the hated. There is no escape except by allowing it when the aspects differ, or by restricting the claim to that which has only one opposite. Up to here is his speech. And his response to the mentioned entailment: For the proof of the entailment of willing a thing to hating its opposite is the necessity of willing both opposites on the assumption of non-entailment, as he will mention now. Such a proof is not established that hating a thing entails willing its opposite, because it is possible to hate both opposites, unlike willing them according to the Ash'aris. His statement: 'Because it is possible that he wills both opposites' — and also it is possible that neither will nor hate attaches to the opposite, like many perceptible things. Know that what the author mentioned about the possibility of willing both opposites is not valid in the context of invalidating the ruling of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali on the mentioned entailment, because it is possible that each of them is also hated in some respect. It is only valid in the context of a response to their proof which the author did not mention, namely: If the opposite of the willed, being perceived, were not hated, it would be willed, so willing both opposites would follow, which is impossible, because the two wills pertaining to the two opposites are mutually contradictory. So reflect.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a kalam discussion on whether willing something necessarily entails hating its opposite. It begins by stating the question: Is will entailed with hatred of the opposite? It clarifies that it is not absolutely entailed because there are cases where they separate. Rather, the disagreement occurs when one is aware of the opposite. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Ghazali hold that it is entailed. The author's apparent view is contrary: a person can will both opposites from one aspect, either equally or with preference for one due to greater benefit, so both are willed but not equally. This shows that will does not entail hate. Then the commentator elaborates on the argument. He explains that if the opposite were not hated, it could be willed, leading to the possibility of willing both opposites, which is impossible. He then responds to an objection that it is possible that neither will nor hate attaches to the opposite. The commentator discusses the proof from Sharh al-Maqasid, showing that the alleged entailment does not hold because one can will both opposites from different aspects. He also notes that the author's example is not a refutation of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali's ruling but rather a response to their unmentioned proof.

مستلزمة
شعور

Correction: The author shows that willing can occur without hatred, e.g., when one wills both opposites from different aspects, or when one is unaware of the opposite.

The Will Does Not Impart a Real Attribute
Introduction of the thesis and the two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Qadi and al-Basri held that the will does not add a real existential attribute to its object, only a relational description.

Core Idea
Will changes moral description, not essence.

This passage introduces the view that the will does not bestow a real attribute upon its object. Instead, it only makes actions obedience or sin, and speech command or threat. The author clarifies that if this meant a real attribute, it would be incorrect; rather it is merely relational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the condition about the definition of will at the start.
The earlier argument about willing opposites only applies to a specific definition of will.
2
Identify the thesis
Find the statement by al-Qadi and al-Basri: 'No will imparts to its object an attribute additional to its essence.'
They give examples for action and speech.
3
Distinguish the two types of attributes
The author says if they meant a positive attribute (thubutiyya), he denies it; if they meant a relational attribute (i'tibari), there is no dispute.
Learn the Arabic terms: صفة ثبوتية and صفة اعتبارية.
4
Reflect on the speech example
Consider why speech cannot have an existential attribute: its entire sentence does not exist at once.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد السابع : الإرادة وتعلقها بالصفة الذي ذكره إنما يتأتى إذا فسرت الإرادة باعتقاد النفع أو ما يتبعه، وأما إذا فسرت بصفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي الفعل مقارنة كما هو رأي الأشاعرة فلا لأن إرادة الضدين تستلزم اجتماعهما معاً. [ المقصد السابع : الإرادة وتعلقها بالصفة ] ( قال القاضي) من الأشاعرة ) وأبو عبد الله البصري) من المعتزلة : ( لا إرادة تفيد متعلقها صفة زائدة على ذات المتعلق سواء كان المتعلق فعلاً أو قولاً، ( فللفعل) تفيد ( كونه طاعة ) كالسجود بإرادته لله تعالى، (ومعصية ) كالسجود بإرادته للصنم، (وللقول) تفيد (كونه أمراً أو تهديداً فإن أرادا) أي القاضي والبصري ( أنها ) أي الإرادة تفيد ) متعلقها (صفة ثبوتية) موجودة في الخارج (منع) كون الإرادة كذلك ( وما ذكراه ) من كون الفعل طاعة أو معصية وكون القول أمراً أو تهديداً وصف (اعتباري) لا تحقق له في الخارج، (كيف والقول لا وجود لجملته) معاً، فكيف تقوم به (صفة وجودية وإن أرادا أنها تفيد متعلقها صفة اعتبارية، فذلك مما لا ينازع فيه ولا يتصور في ذكره مزيد فائدة . قوله : ( كما هو رأي الأشاعرة فإن القصد المتقدم على الفعل يلزم عليه وليس بإرادة كما مر، فظهر ضعف ما في شرح المقاصد من القول بأن متعلق الإرادة الحادثة لا يكون إلا مقارناً الإرادية حتى ما يكون متعلقاً بالمستقبل يكون من قبيل النهي مخالف للغة والعرف والتحقيق . قوله : ( ومنع كون الإرادة (كذلك كيف ولو كانت الإرادة موجبة لصفة وجودية ومؤثرة لها لانقلبت الإرادة قدرة، لثبوت أخص صفة القدرة كذا في أبكار الأفكار .
The third type - the seventh objective: The will and its relation to the attribute. What he mentioned is only applicable if will is interpreted as belief in benefit or what follows it. But if it is interpreted as an attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the action simultaneously, as is the view of the Ash'aris, then it does not apply, because willing both opposites entails their conjunction together. [The seventh objective: The will and its relation to the attribute] (Al-Qadi) from the Ash'aris and Abu Abdullah al-Basri from the Mu'tazilites said: No will imparts to its object an attribute additional to the essence of the object, whether the object is an action or a speech. For an action, it imparts that it is obedience, like prostrating with the intention of Allah, and sin, like prostrating with the intention of an idol. For speech, it imparts that it is a command or a threat. If they (i.e., al-Qadi and al-Basri) meant that the will imparts to its object a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) existing externally, (then) they denied that the will is like that. And what they mentioned – that the action is obedience or sin and the speech is command or threat – is a relational attribute (i'tibari) not realized externally. How can speech, whose entire sentence has no existence together, be characterized by an existential attribute? And if they meant that it imparts a relational attribute, then there is no dispute in that, and no additional benefit can be conceived in mentioning it. His statement: 'As is the view of the Ash'aris, for the intention preceding the action is entailed by it and is not will, as has passed. So the weakness of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid is apparent, from the statement that the object of originated will is only simultaneous with the will, to the extent that what is related to the future is of the type of prohibition, contrary to language, custom, and verification.' His statement: 'And he denied that the will is like that.' Why? If the will necessitated an existential attribute and influenced it, the will would turn into power, because the most specific attribute of power is established. Thus in Abkar al-Afkar.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage discusses whether the will imparts an attribute to its object. It begins by noting that the previous argument about willing opposites only applies if will is defined as belief in benefit or its consequence; under the Ash'ari definition of will as a specifying attribute simultaneous with the action, it fails. Then it presents the view of al-Qadi (an Ash'ari) and Abu Abdullah al-Basri (a Mu'tazilite): the will does not give an additional attribute to its object. For actions, will makes them obedience or sin; for speech, it makes them command or threat. If they meant this as a real (thubutiyya) attribute, the author denies it. These designations are merely relational (i'tibari) and not externally real. He argues that a speech act, whose entire sentence does not exist simultaneously, cannot sustain a real attribute. If they meant only a relational attribute, then there is no disagreement and no benefit in mentioning it. The commentator then addresses a point from Sharh al-Maqasid about the simultaneity of will and its object, criticizing it as contrary to language and verification. Finally, he argues that if will imparted a real attribute, it would become power (qudra), because that is the specific attribute of power.

Key Terms
إرادة
صفة ثبوتية
صفة اعتبارية
طاعة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the will changes the object's essence by adding a real quality.
Correction: The will only gives a conventional description (اعتباري) without any real change in the object.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi and al-Basri, what kind of attribute does the will impart to its object?
A) A real/existential attribute (صفة ثبوتية).
B) A relational/considerative attribute (صفة اعتبارية).
C) No attribute at all.
D) A physical attribute.

Key Takeaway
The will only adds a relational attribute, not an existential one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you ever assume that your intention automatically makes the act praiseworthy regardless of its outer form? This lesson teaches that will only adds a conventional label, not a real quality. Are you relying more on your intention than on the actual substance of your deeds? How will you examine your actions to ensure they are truly obedient, not just labeled as such by your will?
?
The example of prostrating to Allah vs. to an idol shows that the same physical action can be obedience or sin based on will. Think of a time you did something that could be interpreted as good or bad depending on intention. Did you evaluate your own heart before acting? Knowing that your will does not change the reality of the act, how can you be more vigilant about your intentions in daily decisions?
?
If speech like 'command' or 'threat' is only a relational attribute and not real, then your words carry moral weight only by convention. Do you sometimes speak carelessly, assuming your intention justifies the words? This lesson forces you to consider that your words are not inherently good or evil; they are judged by their relational context. Will you now be more cautious about what you say, realizing that the same sentence can be a command or a threat depending on your will and the context?

Why the Will Cannot Bestow an Existential Attribute
Arguments against will imparting a real attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that if the will gave a real attribute, it would become power, and that speech cannot sustain an existential attribute.

Core Idea
If will gave a real attribute, it would be power.

In this second part, the author provides two further arguments against the view that the will imparts a real attribute. First, he criticizes the notion that the object of will must be simultaneous with it, citing language and custom. Second, he argues that if will caused an existential attribute, it would become indistinguishable from power, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two sentences starting with 'His statement:'.
The first part criticizes a view from Sharh al-Maqasid.
2
Analyze the power argument
Notice the conditional: if will necessitated an existential attribute, then will would turn into power.
Power's most specific attribute is that it gives existence to an act.
3
Understand the objection to simultaneity
The author rejects the claim that the object of originated will must be simultaneous, using future acts as a counterexample.
This is in line with the Ash'ari view that will can pertain to future acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد السابع : الإرادة وتعلقها بالصفة الذي ذكره إنما يتأتى إذا فسرت الإرادة باعتقاد النفع أو ما يتبعه، وأما إذا فسرت بصفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي الفعل مقارنة كما هو رأي الأشاعرة فلا لأن إرادة الضدين تستلزم اجتماعهما معاً. [ المقصد السابع : الإرادة وتعلقها بالصفة ] ( قال القاضي) من الأشاعرة ) وأبو عبد الله البصري) من المعتزلة : ( لا إرادة تفيد متعلقها صفة زائدة على ذات المتعلق سواء كان المتعلق فعلاً أو قولاً، ( فللفعل) تفيد ( كونه طاعة ) كالسجود بإرادته لله تعالى، (ومعصية ) كالسجود بإرادته للصنم، (وللقول) تفيد (كونه أمراً أو تهديداً فإن أرادا) أي القاضي والبصري ( أنها ) أي الإرادة تفيد ) متعلقها (صفة ثبوتية) موجودة في الخارج (منع) كون الإرادة كذلك ( وما ذكراه ) من كون الفعل طاعة أو معصية وكون القول أمراً أو تهديداً وصف (اعتباري) لا تحقق له في الخارج، (كيف والقول لا وجود لجملته) معاً، فكيف تقوم به (صفة وجودية وإن أرادا أنها تفيد متعلقها صفة اعتبارية، فذلك مما لا ينازع فيه ولا يتصور في ذكره مزيد فائدة . قوله : ( كما هو رأي الأشاعرة فإن القصد المتقدم على الفعل يلزم عليه وليس بإرادة كما مر، فظهر ضعف ما في شرح المقاصد من القول بأن متعلق الإرادة الحادثة لا يكون إلا مقارناً الإرادية حتى ما يكون متعلقاً بالمستقبل يكون من قبيل النهي مخالف للغة والعرف والتحقيق . قوله : ( ومنع كون الإرادة (كذلك كيف ولو كانت الإرادة موجبة لصفة وجودية ومؤثرة لها لانقلبت الإرادة قدرة، لثبوت أخص صفة القدرة كذا في أبكار الأفكار .
The third type - the seventh objective: The will and its relation to the attribute. What he mentioned is only applicable if will is interpreted as belief in benefit or what follows it. But if it is interpreted as an attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the action simultaneously, as is the view of the Ash'aris, then it does not apply, because willing both opposites entails their conjunction together. [The seventh objective: The will and its relation to the attribute] (Al-Qadi) from the Ash'aris and Abu Abdullah al-Basri from the Mu'tazilites said: No will imparts to its object an attribute additional to the essence of the object, whether the object is an action or a speech. For an action, it imparts that it is obedience, like prostrating with the intention of Allah, and sin, like prostrating with the intention of an idol. For speech, it imparts that it is a command or a threat. If they (i.e., al-Qadi and al-Basri) meant that the will imparts to its object a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) existing externally, (then) they denied that the will is like that. And what they mentioned – that the action is obedience or sin and the speech is command or threat – is a relational attribute (i'tibari) not realized externally. How can speech, whose entire sentence has no existence together, be characterized by an existential attribute? And if they meant that it imparts a relational attribute, then there is no dispute in that, and no additional benefit can be conceived in mentioning it. His statement: 'As is the view of the Ash'aris, for the intention preceding the action is entailed by it and is not will, as has passed. So the weakness of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid is apparent, from the statement that the object of originated will is only simultaneous with the will, to the extent that what is related to the future is of the type of prohibition, contrary to language, custom, and verification.' His statement: 'And he denied that the will is like that.' Why? If the will necessitated an existential attribute and influenced it, the will would turn into power, because the most specific attribute of power is established. Thus in Abkar al-Afkar.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage discusses whether the will imparts an attribute to its object. It begins by noting that the previous argument about willing opposites only applies if will is defined as belief in benefit or its consequence; under the Ash'ari definition of will as a specifying attribute simultaneous with the action, it fails. Then it presents the view of al-Qadi (an Ash'ari) and Abu Abdullah al-Basri (a Mu'tazilite): the will does not give an additional attribute to its object. For actions, will makes them obedience or sin; for speech, it makes them command or threat. If they meant this as a real (thubutiyya) attribute, the author denies it. These designations are merely relational (i'tibari) and not externally real. He argues that a speech act, whose entire sentence does not exist simultaneously, cannot sustain a real attribute. If they meant only a relational attribute, then there is no disagreement and no benefit in mentioning it. The commentator then addresses a point from Sharh al-Maqasid about the simultaneity of will and its object, criticizing it as contrary to language and verification. Finally, he argues that if will imparted a real attribute, it would become power (qudra), because that is the specific attribute of power.

Misconception: That the object of will must always be simultaneous with the will (as some claimed for originated will).
Correction: Will can relate to future acts, as is well known in language and custom.

If will gave a real attribute, it would become power—an impossible confusion of divine attributes.

Definition of Created Power as Facility and Its Proof
Commentator defines taysir and explains the proof of power from differential facility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of created power as facility (taysir) and how differential facility proves the existence of power in an agent.

Core Idea
Facility of an act from one agent and its difficulty from another proves power.

The commentator explains that created power is known through observing that some beings can easily perform an act while others cannot. This differential facility points to the presence of power in the capable agent. The passage also clarifies that taysir includes even difficult acts and aligns with the Mu'tazili conception of power as being for the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the concise style of the author.
The passage consists of two sentences: a definition/proof and a commentary on the term taysir.
2
Understand the definition of taysir
Identify how taysir (facility) is defined: it is readiness, the opposite of difficulty. Note that it applies even to difficult acts.
The commentator stresses that taysir is not the opposite of itself but of difficulty.
3
Grasp the proof
The proof from differential facility: if we observe that an act is easy for one existent and difficult for another, we infer that the first has power and the second does not.
This reasoning relies on observable differences in ease of action.
4
Connect to Mu'tazili theology
The passage notes that for the Mu'tazila, power is specifically for the act, so the evidence for prior power is the facility of the act and the readiness of its opposite to occur.
This highlights the empirical basis for inferring power in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تيسره ( من بعض الموجودين دون (بعض) فإذا علمنا تيسر فعل من موجود وتعذره من غيره علمنا أن الأول له قدرة دون الثاني. قوله : (أي تيسره من تيسر الأمر أي تهيأ ضد تعذر لا مقابل نفسه، فيتناول الاختبارات العسرة أيضاً، وإنما فسر بذلك لأن القدرة عند المعتزلة على الفعل، فالدليل على ثبوت القدرة السابقة تيسير الفعل وتهيأ ضده لحصوله، فإنه يدل على وجودها مع الفعل.
Its facility from some existent things without others. So if we know the facility of an act from an existent and its difficulty from another, we know that the first has power without the second. His statement: meaning its facility from the facility of the thing, i.e., readiness, the opposite of difficulty, not the opposite of itself, so it includes difficult acts as well. It is interpreted that way because power according to the Mu'tazila is for the act, so the evidence for the existence of prior power is the facility of the act and the readiness of its opposite to occur, for it indicates its existence with the act.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines and proves created power. The first sentence states the definition: when we see that one being can easily perform an act while another cannot, we know the first has power. The commentator then explains that 'taysir' (facility) means readiness, the opposite of difficulty, and it includes even difficult acts. He notes that for the Mu'tazila, power is for the act, so the proof of power is the facility of the act and the readiness of its opposite, indicating that power exists with the act.

Correction: The passage shows that power is inferred from the facility (ease) of the act compared to another agent's difficulty, not from the act's mere occurrence.

Refutation of Objections to the Facility Proof of Power
Refutation of three objections: the prevented one, al-Hamdani's definition, and power as qualifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main objections to the facility proof of power and how they are refuted, clarifying the distinction between a prevented person and an incapable person.

Core Idea
Prevention does not negate power; the qualifier distinguishes the capable from the incapable.

The facility proof of power argues that power is known through the ease or possibility of action. However, critics raise three objections: the prevented person seems powerful yet cannot act, al-Hamdani's definition of power as 'proceeding of the act' is ambiguous, and power as a qualifier rather than necessitator seems to conflate the prevented with the incapable. This lesson examines each objection and its deft refutation, clarifying the precise nature of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Note that the passage contains three distinct objections and their refutations. Try to outline them.
The objections are presented in a dialectical format: objection (قلنا or فإن قال) followed by refutation.
2
Analyze the first objection: the prevented person
The first objection claims that a prevented person (e.g., handcuffed) is considered powerful, so the act's not proceeding does not disprove power. The refutation shows that the same reasoning applies to the incapable if we remove incapability, undermining the objection.
3
Examine the second objection: al-Hamdani's definition
Al-Hamdani defines power as 'the proceeding of the act' (ta'atti). The refutation clarifies three possible meanings of 'proceeding' and shows that only the last (dependence on will) is relevant, and it is not a circular definition.
The commentator also addresses a subtlety about change in attributes.
4
Understand the third objection: power as qualifier (musahhih)
The third objection argues that power qualifies the act but does not necessitate it, so the prevented person has the qualifier while the incapable does not. The refutation points out that if we remove both obstacles, both can act, so there is no basis for the distinction.
The text concludes that the distinction is intuitive and undeniable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : (الممنوع من الفعل ( قادر عندك ) على الفعل ومعلوم قدرته عليه ( ولا يتأتى منه الفعل حال كونه ممنوعاً منه، بل يتعذر عليه، فلا يختص طريق إثباتها يتأتى الفعل ( فإن قال : ( الهمداني ( يتأتى ) الفعل (منه) أي الممنوع ) بتقدير ارتفاع المانع قلنا فالعاجز يتأتى منه الفعل بتقدير ارتفاع المانع وهو العجز فيلزم أن يكون العاجز قادراً فإن قال : القدرة مصححة للفعل لا موجبة له ولا شك أن الممنوع موصوف بما يصححه، إلا أنه تخلف عنه لأجل المانع بخلاف العاجز، إذ ليس معه ما يصحح الفعل، قلنا : المعلوم بلا شبهة هو أن الفعل يتعذر عليهما ما داما على حالهما وإذا فرض زوال ما بهما يتأتى الفعل منهما، فمن أين لك وجود المصحح مع أحدهما دون الآخر؟ قوله : ( فلا يختص إلخ ) بل لا بد في إثباتها الممنوع من طريق آخر فالباء داخلة على المقصور. قوله : ( وقال الهمداني من المعتزلة هو تأتي الفعل إلخ) اعترض عليه بأنه إن أراد بالتأتي الوجود والامتناع ننقض ببرودة الماء ونحوه، وإن أراد السهولة ننقض بالاختبارات العسيرة وإن أراد الفعل إن شاء يتوقف على المشيئة والاختيار فهو فرع القدرة وأجيب بأن المراد هو المعنى الأخير، فلا فرعية بحسب العلم. قوله : (قلنا : الممنوع من الفعل إلخ) أجيب : بأن مراده تأتي الفعل من البعض وهو بحالة ذاته وصفاته فيندفع الممنوع بلا نقض بالعاجز، لأن تأتي الفعل من العاجز عند تغيره من صفة إلى صفة، وأما الممنوع فالتغير عند قدرته في أمر من خارج وتقرير الشارح يشير إلى دفعه فتأمل. قوله : ( فمن أين لك وجود المصحح إلخ) قيل : الفرق العاجز رأساً وبين بين الممنوع من الفعل من أظهر الوجدانيات لا يتوقف العقل في الجزم به فإنكاره مما يقضي منه العجب.
We said: The one prevented from the act is, according to you, powerful over the act, and his power over it is known, yet the act does not proceed from him while he is prevented, rather it is impossible for him, so the way of proving it is not exclusive to the act proceeding. For if he said: al-Hamdani: the act proceeds from him (i.e., the prevented one) on the supposition of the removal of the obstacle. We said: The incapable also has the act proceeding from him on the supposition of the removal of the obstacle, which is incapability, so it would follow that the incapable is powerful. For if he said: Power is a qualifier for the act, not a necessitator, and there is no doubt that the prevented is described with what qualifies it, except that it is not actualized due to the obstacle, unlike the incapable, for he does not have what qualifies the act. We said: What is known without doubt is that the act is impossible for both as long as they are in their states, and if the removal of what is with them is supposed, the act proceeds from both. So whence do you have the existence of the qualifier with one without the other? His statement: not exclusive etc. Rather, to prove it for the prevented, another way is necessary, so the ب is for the restricted. His statement: and al-Hamdani from the Mu'tazila said it is the proceeding of the act etc. It was objected that if he means by proceeding existence and non-existence, it is refuted by the coldness of water and the like; if he means ease, it is refuted by difficult acts; if he means the act if he wills, it depends on will and choice, so it is a branch of power. It was answered that what is intended is the last meaning, so it is not a branch according to knowledge. His statement: We said: the prevented from the act etc. It was answered that his intention is the proceeding of the act from some, while he is in his essence and attributes, so the objection of the prevented is repelled without refutation by the incapable, because the proceeding of the act from the incapable is when he changes from one attribute to another, whereas for the prevented, the change is due to his power over something external. The commentator's expression indicates its repulsion, so ponder. His statement: So whence do you have the existence of the qualifier etc. It was said: The difference between the incapable and the prevented from the act is among the most evident intuitive matters; the intellect does not hesitate to judge it, and its denial is among the most astonishing things.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the proof of power from facility and their refutations. The first objection: the one prevented from acting (like a handcuffed person) is still considered powerful, so proof cannot be based on the act proceeding. The answer: if we suppose removal of the obstacle, the act would proceed from him, but the same applies to the incapable if we suppose removal of incapability, so the objection fails. The second objection from al-Hamdani: power is defined as the act's proceeding (ta'atti) from the agent. It is objected that 'proceeding' could mean existence, ease, or dependence on will; only the last is valid, but it is a branch of power. The answer: the intended meaning is the last, but it is not a branch according to knowledge (i.e., we can know power through it). The third objection: power is a qualifier (musahhih) not a necessitator (mujib), so the prevented has the qualifier. The answer: if we suppose removal of both obstacles, both become capable, so there is no distinction. The commentator notes that the difference between the incapable and the prevented is intuitive and undeniable.

ممنوع
عاجز
مصحح
موجب
تأتي الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a prevented person (e.g., handcuffed) lacks power because they cannot act.
Correction: The text clarifies that the prevented person is powerful but the act is hindered by an external obstacle; the incapable lacks the internal qualifier for action.

Checkpoint
How does the text refute the objection that the prevented one has power because the act proceeds from him when the obstacle is removed?
A) By showing that the incapable also has the act proceed when incapability is removed
B) By denying the premise
C) By saying that power is a qualifier
D) By appealing to intuition

Key Takeaway
The facility proof of power withstands objections by showing that prevention does not negate power, that al-Hamdani's definition is salvageable, and that the qualifier distinguishes the incapable from the prevented in an intuitive manner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim you cannot do a good deed, are you truly incapable (like the 'ajiz) or just prevented (like the mamnu') by your own laziness, fear, or distractions? What would change if you honestly admitted that the obstacle is external and removable?
?
Al-Hamdani defined power as the act proceeding if one wills. Do you will to obey Allah but then excuse yourself by saying you are prevented? This lesson forces you to ask: Is the prevention real, or is it a cover for a lack of true will?
?
The commentator says the difference between the prevented and the incapable is intuitive. When you reflect on a missed obligation, do you intuitively sense whether it was due to genuine inability or merely external hindrances? Refutation of al-Jubba'i's Definition of Power
Refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition of power as knowledge of soundness and safety from afflictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jubba'i's equation of power with soundness and safety from afflictions is refuted, especially through the examples of sleep and incapacity.

Core Idea
Soundness does not guarantee power; a sleeping person is sound yet powerless.

In defining power (qudra), al-Jubba'i argued that it is known through a person's physical soundness and safety from afflictions. However, this definition fails because soundness can exist without power. This lesson examines the refutation based on the cases of sleep and incapacity, showing that soundness alone is insufficient for attributing power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the structure: al-Jubba'i's claim, followed by a counterargument.
Pay attention to the terms sihha (soundness) and qudra (power).
2
Identify the counterexample
The passage uses the example of a person who is sound but asleep or incapacitated. Such a person is not considered powerful for certain acts like flying. Identify how this refutes al-Jubba'i.
3
Understand the defense of sleep as non-affliction
The commentator argues that sleep is actually evidence of soundness, not an affliction. Consider why this matters for the definition.
This counters an objection from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علي ( الجبائي : هو ( أي طريق العلم بالقدرة ( العلم بصحة الشخص) وسلامته عن الآفات (قلنا: قد توجد تلك الصحة للشخص ) ولا قدرة له عند اتصافه بأضدادها من النوم والعجز فلا يكون العلم بتلك الصحة مستلزماً للعلم بثبوت القدرة، كيف والصحيح المتصف بتلك الأضداد لا قدرة له (اجماعاً ) . قوله: ( وقد يوجد إلخ) يعني أن الصحة توجد للشخص عند اتصافه بالنوم والعجز بالنسبة إلى بعض الأفعال كالطيران فيقال : إنه ليس بقادر عليه ولا يقال : إنه ليس بصحيح كيف والنوم دليل الصحة فاندفع ما في شرح المقاصد من أنه يمكن أن يقال النوم آفة.
Ali al-Jubba'i: It (i.e., the way of knowing power) is knowledge of the person's soundness and his safety from afflictions. We said: That soundness may exist for the person, yet he has no power when he is described by its opposites, such as sleep and incapacity. So knowledge of that soundness is not necessitating knowledge of the existence of power. How is that? The sound person described by those opposites has no power by consensus. His statement: (and it may exist etc.) means that soundness exists for the person when he is described by sleep and incapacity with respect to some acts, such as flying; so it is said that he is not capable of it, but it is not said that he is not sound. How? Sleep is evidence of soundness. Thus what is in Sharh al-Maqasid that it could be said that sleep is an affliction is repelled.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Jubba'i's definition of power as knowledge of a person's soundness and safety from afflictions. The refutation argues that soundness can exist without power, for example, when a person is asleep or incapacitated. Such a person is sound (healthy) yet has no power over certain acts, like flying. The commentator adds that sleep is actually evidence of soundness, not an affliction, countering an objection from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Key Takeaway
Soundness and safety from afflictions are not sufficient conditions for power, as a sound person may still lack power due to sleep or incapacity.

Created Power is Simultaneous with the Act
Ash'ari view that created power exists only at the moment of the act, not before.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that created power exists only at the moment of the act, and the logical argument used to support it.

Core Idea
Created power cannot precede the act; it arises simultaneously with it.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari doctrine on the timing of created power. The passage articulates their view that power exists simultaneously with the act, and provides a reductio proof that it cannot exist before. We'll examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the core claim and the argument.
Notice the compact style of the Arabic; the translation clarifies the logic.
2
Identify the reductio argument structure
Look for the conditional reasoning: 'if power existed before the act, then...' and the conclusion that this leads to a contradiction.
The argument uses the impossibility of two contradictories coexisting.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this position matters in Ash'ari theology: it emphasizes that created agents have no power before the moment of action, highlighting divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( قال الشيخ ) وأصحابه: (القدرة ) الحادثة (مع الفعل) أي أنها توجد حال حدوث الفعل، وتتعلق به في هذه الحالة ( ولا توجد ( القدرة الحادثة (قبله) فضلا عن تعلقها به (إذ قبل الفعل لا يمكن (الفعل) . بل يمتنع وجوده فيه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم. قوله: (أي إنها توجد إلخ ليس المراد مجرد مقارنتها بالفعل فإنه لا نزاع فيه بل في وجودها حال حدوث الفعل وتعلقها به في تلك الحالة فإن المعتزلة قائلون بحدوثها وتعلقها قبل حدوثها. قوله : ( إذ قبل الفعل إلخ ) تقريرة على قانون الاستدلال أن يقال : لو وجد القدرة الحادثة قبل الفعل في وقت معين لكان الفعل مقدوراً فيه بالنسبة إلى تلك القدرة والتالي باطل، أما الملازمة فظاهرة إذ لا قدرة بدون .المقدور وأما بطلان التاليق لأن الفعل في ذلك الوقت لو كان ممكناً فيه فليفرض وقوعه، فيكون الحال السابقة على أن الفعل حال تقدمها غير مقدمة عليه، فيلزم إمكان اجتماع النقيضين هذا تقرير الكلام، بحيث يحتلي عروس المرام ويدفع الشكوك
Al-Shaykh and his companions said: Created power is with the act, i.e., it exists at the moment of the act's occurrence and is related to it in that state, and created power does not exist before it, let alone its relation to it, for before the act, the act is not possible, rather its existence in it is impossible; otherwise (i.e., if it were not impossible). His statement: i.e., it exists, etc. The intended meaning is not mere concomitance with the act, for there is no dispute about that, but rather its existence at the moment of the act's occurrence and its relation to it in that state, for the Mu'tazila claim its occurrence and relation before its occurrence. His statement: for before the act, etc. Its formulation according to the method of inference is that it should be said: If created power existed before the act at a specific time, then the act would be possible in that time with respect to that power—and the consequent is false. As for the implication, it is obvious, for there is no power without the thing over which it has power. As for the falsity of the consequent, it is because if the act were possible in that time, let its occurrence be supposed; then the state preceding the act would be that the act in its state of priority is not prior to it, so the possibility of the conjunction of two contradictories would follow. This is the formulation of the speech, such that it adorns the bride of the desire and repels doubts.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrine that created power exists simultaneously with the act, not before. The commentator clarifies that the dispute is not about mere concomitance but about whether power exists and is related before the act. The proof is a reductio: if power existed before the act, the act would be possible at that time. But if it were possible, we could suppose its occurrence, leading to a contradiction: the act would be both prior and not prior to itself. Thus, power cannot precede the act. This argument is presented as a strong logical proof.

Key Terms
قدرة حادثة
مع الفعل
مقدور
امتناع
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Created power exists as a potential before the act, enabling the agent to choose.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that created power comes into existence only simultaneously with the act, not before. Before the act, the act is not possible (impossible) from the creature's side.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position regarding the temporal relationship between created power and the act?
A) Power exists before the act
B) Power exists after the act
C) Power exists simultaneously with the act
D) Power is eternal

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris affirm that created power exists only at the moment of the act, supported by a reductio argument that power before the act would lead to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you are trying to give up. Do you ever say 'I'll stop tomorrow'? According to this lesson, your power to avoid that sin exists only when you actually avoid it. What does that mean for your intention to delay repentance?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you attribute the ability to your own potential? How does the Ash'ari position that your power arises with the act itself shift your sense of gratitude and dependence on Allah?
?
If your power to act is not a stored resource but appears at the moment of action, how might that change your approach to prayer? Will you begin each act of worship with a direct plea to Allah for that momentary power? Be specific about one concrete change you will make starting today.
