Geometric Illustration of Quwwa as Power of a Line
Geometric explanation of quwwa as the power of a line with Pythagorean theorem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term quwwa is used in geometry to denote the power of a line, specifically in relation to the Pythagorean theorem.

Core Idea
The square of a line is its power; the chord's square equals sum of squares of sides.

In addition to its philosophical meaning, quwwa is used in geometry to describe the power of a line. This lesson explores that usage through the Pythagorean theorem and explains the two meanings of 'square.'

Guided Path
1
Read the engineers' statement
Read the statement that the square of a line is its power, and the chord's square equals the squares of the sides.
This is the starting point of the geometric illustration.
2
Understand the two meanings of murabba'
Murabba' can mean a numerical square or a geometric square. Both are relevant.
3
Follow the numerical example
The example uses sides of 10. Compute squares and find the chord's square as 200.
The chord's root is between 14 and 15.
4
See the equality for both meanings
The Pythagorean equality holds for both numerical and geometric squares. Contemplate this.

?

Does Ibn Sina's Definition of Quwwa Include Passive Power?
Examines whether Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa covers both active and passive powers, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as 'principle of change in another' includes passive power, and the key arguments on both sides.

Core Idea
The phrase 'in another' may restrict quwwa to active power, but some interpret it broadly.

Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa (power) is a foundational concept in Islamic philosophy and kalam. However, there is a dispute about whether it covers both active and passive powers. This passage from the commentary examines the objection that the phrase 'in another' limits the definition to active power, and considers the counterargument that 'otherness' can be relative. Understanding this debate clarifies the precise scope of quwwa as a technical term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the translation. Pay attention to the flow: the commentator first states the wider usage, then raises an objection, quotes support, and presents a discussion.
Notice the structure: inclusive definition -> objection -> support -> criticism -> counter.
2
Identify the two sides
Find the commentator's argument that 'in another' implies a distinction between the locus of the principle and the locus of change. This seems to favor active power.
3
Reflect on the counterargument
Consider the possibility that 'otherness' is relative to the agent, allowing inclusion of passive power. Ask yourself which interpretation seems stronger based on the text.
The passage leaves the matter open for discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مبدأ التغير في آخر إلخ ) قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : القوة بحسب الاصطلاح يتناول القوة الفعلية والانفعالية أعني التي تهيأ محلها نحو الفعل أو الانفعال فلذلك أخذوا في تعريفها التغير الشامل للفعل والانفعال، وقال أيضاً : فإن قلت : هل يطلق القوة المؤثرة على القوة الانفعالية . قلت : صرح بذلك بعضهم وادعى أنهم أرادوا بالتأثير هاهنا التغير مطلقاً سواء كان تأثيراً أو تأثراً، ثم قال بعد نقل قول الكاتبي : القوة بمعنى الصفة المؤثرة عرفها الشيخ بأنها مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر وهذا الكلام منه يؤيد إطلاق التأثير على المعنى المتناول للتأثير والقبول كما مر فقد ظهر لك من سياق كلامه أنه حمل التغير في تعريف الشيخ على المعنى الشامل للتأثير والتأثر وجعل التعريف شاملاً للقوة الفعلية والانفعالية، وفيه بحث لأن التغير بحسب مفهومه وإن كان أعم، إلا أن اعتبار كون ذلك التغير في آخر يدل على تخصيص التعريف بالقوة الفعلية لأن المتبادر هو المغايرة بين محل المبدأ ومحل التغير ولو بالاعتبار كما لا يخفى، اللهم إلا أن يقال : الآخرية معتبرة بالنسبة إلى الفاعل مطلقاً فحاصل التعريف أنها مبدأ التغير من شيء في آخر .
His saying: 'A principle of change in another etc.' The commentator said in the glosses of al-Tajrīd: 'Al-quwwa according to technical usage includes active quwwa and passive quwwa, i.e., that which prepares its subject for action or passion. Therefore they took in its definition change (taghayyur) inclusive of action and passion.' And he also said: 'If you say: Is al-quwwa al-mu’aththira (influential power) applied to al-quwwa al-infi‘āliyya (passive power)? I say: Some have explicitly stated that and claimed that by 'influence' here they meant change absolutely, whether it be influence or being influenced.' Then he said after quoting the words of al-Kātibī: 'Al-quwwa in the sense of the influential attribute was defined by the Shaykh [Ibn Sīnā] as a principle of change in another insofar as it is another, and this speech from him supports applying influence to the meaning that includes influence and reception as has passed. So it has appeared to you from the context of his speech that he interpreted change in the Shaykh's definition as including influence and being influenced, and he made the definition inclusive of active and passive power. And in this there is discussion, because change according to its concept, although more general, yet the consideration that that change is in another indicates a specification of the definition to active power, because the obvious meaning is the otherness (al-mughāyara) between the locus of the principle and the locus of change, even if by consideration, as is not hidden. However, it could be said that the otherness is considered relative to the agent absolutely, so the summary of the definition is that it is a principle of change from something in another.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion about the scope of Ibn Sīnā's definition of quwwa. The commentator begins by stating that quwwa in technical usage includes both active and passive powers, and that the definition should be taken to include change (taghayyur) broadly covering action and passion. He then raises an objection: 'Is the term 'influential quwwa' applied to passive quwwa?' Answer: Yes, some have explicitly said so, claiming that 'influence' here means change absolutely. To support this, he quotes al-Kātibī, who reports that the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) defined quwwa as 'principle of change in another' and that this supports the inclusive interpretation. However, the commentator then presents a discussion (baḥth): although the concept of change is general, the stipulation 'in another' indicates that the definition is specific to active power, because it implies a distinction between the locus of the principle and the locus of change—they are different even if only in consideration. Nonetheless, he offers a possible counter: 'otherness' can be considered relative to the agent absolutely, so the definition could be read as 'principle of change from something in another,' which might accommodate both. Thus, the passage leaves the matter open, with the objection that the definition seems restricted to active power and the response that the otherness might be relative.

Key Terms
قوة فعالية
قوة انفعالية
تأثير
تأثر
تغير
الشيخ
آخرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as 'principle of change in another' clearly includes both active and passive powers.
Correction: The phrase 'in another' suggests a distinction between the locus of the principle and the locus of change, which may restrict it to active power; the inclusion of passive power is a matter of interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main objection to including passive power in Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa?
A) Passive power does not involve change.
B) The definition says 'in another', implying the locus of the principle differs from the locus of change.
C) Ibn Sina explicitly excluded passive power.
D) Change cannot be attributed to inanimate objects.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of passive power in Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa is contentious because the phrase 'in another' suggests a distinction between cause and effect that applies primarily to active power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you usually think of your 'power' (quwwa) as only your ability to act and influence, or are you equally aware of your capacity to be changed and affected by truth and by Allah? Examine a recent instance where you had to choose between asserting your influence or being receptive to correction.
?
The commentator notes that some interpret 'in another' as referring to the agent only. In your spiritual life, do you tend to view God as the sole agent and yourself merely passive, or do you balance between active striving and receptive submission? How does this lesson challenge your default posture?
?
If the definition of quwwa were to include only active power, what would that say about your readiness to accept influence from the Quran and the scholars? Are you more comfortable being the one who changes things, or being changed by the truth? Pause and ask Allah to make you soft to His guidance.

Defining Quwwa as the Genus of Capability
Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa and its explanation with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as 'the principle of change in another insofar as it is another' and how it functions as the genus for capability, with the example of the self-treating physician.

Core Idea
Quwwa is the principle of change in another, encompassing both acting and being acted upon.

This lesson introduces the precise definition of power (quwwa) that is foundational for the discussion of capability (qudra). Ibn Sina's formulation pinpoints the essence of power as a source of change in something else, and the commentator clarifies its scope with an illustrative example that includes cases where the agent and patient are the same entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that states the definition of quwwa and its intended meaning as the genus of capability. Notice the phrasing 'مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر' and the condition 'من حيث هو آخر'.
The definition is concise; every word is significant.
2
Understand the example of the self-treating physician
Consider why Ibn Sina adds 'insofar as it is another' – to include cases like a physician treating himself, where he acts as a physician on himself as patient. This shows quwwa involves both action and passion.
3
Recognize the different meanings of quwwa
The commentator lists other usages of quwwa (possibility, capability, passivity) that are treated elsewhere. This helps to isolate the specific meaning under discussion.
Only the meaning as genus of capability is focused on here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ( القوة تقال للقدرة والمراد هاهنا (جنسها) أي المقصود في المقصد بيان القوة التي هي جنس القدرة ( وهو ) كما قاله ابن سينا : ( مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر وقولنا : من حيث هو آخر ليدخل فيه ) أي في هذا الحد ) المعالج لنفسه فإنه يؤثر من حيث هو عالم بصناعة الطب ( عامل بمقتضاها ) ويتأثر

قوله : ( القوة تقال للقدرة إلخ ) فبهذه المناسبة أورد بيان معاني القوة في بحث القدرة . قوله : ( يعني أن المقصود إلخ بخلاف المعاني الآخر فإن بيانها موكول في المقاصد الأخرى، وذكرها هاهنا استطرادي لبيان إطلاقات القوة فإن بيان الإمكان قد مر في الأمور العامة والقدرة قد مر في المقاصد السابقة، والانفعال سيجيء في الكيفيات الاستعدادية.

وأرادوا أن مربعه يساوي مربعيهما والقريب أن مجموع مربعيهما، حينئذ يكون قوة للوتر فيندرج في قولهم: مربع الخط قوة له وهذا الكلام يستدعي نوع توضيح فنقول : وتر القائمة له، الواصل بين ضلعيها والمربع قد يطلق على العدد الحاصل من ضرب عدد في نفسه، مثلا إذا ضرب عشرة في نفسها حصل مئة فالمئة مربع العشرة والعشرة جذر المئة، وقد يطلق على سطح يحيط به أربعة أضلاع متساوية، وهو المراد هاهنا كما هو الظاهر ومساواة مربع الوتر بمربعي الضلعين يستقيم على كلا المعنيين فلنتصور على المعنى الأول ليتضح على المعنى الثاني أيضاً فنقول : إذا فرضنا كلا من ضلعي القائمة عشرة أجزاء متساوية فمربع كل ضلع مئة مثلها لأنها الحاصلة من ضرب عشرة في نفسها ومربع الوتر يساوي مربع مجموع الضلعين، أعني المئتين فالوتر جذر مئتين وإنه فوق أربعة عشر جزءا وأقل من خمسة عشر وذلك لأن الحاصل من ضرب أربعة عشر في نفسها مئة وستة وتسعون من ضرب خمسة عشر في نفسها مئتان وخسمة وعشرون فلا بد أن يكون جذر المئتين فيما بينهما، وإذا تخيلت هذا تخيلت المساواة على المعنى الثاني أيضاً فليتأمل.
We say: (The term 'power' is said for capability and what is meant here is (its genus), i.e., the intended [matter] in the objective is the explanation of the power that is the genus of capability. And it is, as Ibn Sina said: (the principle of change in another insofar as it is another. And our saying 'insofar as it is another' is to include in it, i.e., in this definition, the one who treats himself, for he acts insofar as he is knowledgeable in the art of medicine and acts according to it, and he is acted upon.)

His saying: (The term 'power' is said for capability, etc.) On this occasion, he presented the explanation of the meanings of power in the discussion of capability. His saying: (He means that the intended [matter], etc.) Unlike the other meanings, for their explanation is delegated to other objectives, and their mention here is digressive to explain the usages of power, for the explanation of possibility has already passed in the general matters, and capability has passed in the previous objectives, and passivity will come in the dispositonal qualities.

And they intended that its square equals the square of the two [sides] and the approximate [meaning] is that the sum of the two squares, then it is a power for the hypotenuse, so it falls under their saying: the square of a line is a power for it. This speech requires some clarification. We say: The hypotenuse of a right angle connects its two sides. The term 'square' may be applied to the number resulting from multiplying a number by itself, e.g., if ten is multiplied by itself, a hundred results; so a hundred is the square of ten, and ten is the root of a hundred. And it may be applied to a surface bounded by four equal sides, which is what is meant here as is apparent. And the equality of the square of the hypotenuse to the squares of the two sides holds on both meanings. So let us conceptualize it according to the first meaning to make it clear also according to the second meaning. We say: If we assume each of the two sides of the right angle to be ten equal parts, then the square of each side is one hundred like them, because it results from multiplying ten by itself. And the square of the hypotenuse equals the square of the sum of the two sides, i.e., two hundred. So the hypotenuse is the root of two hundred, and it is above fourteen parts and less than fifteen, because the product of fourteen multiplied by itself is one hundred ninety-six, and the product of fifteen multiplied by itself is two hundred twenty-five. So the root of two hundred must be between them. And if you have imagined this, you have imagined the equality on the second meaning as well, so reflect.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'quwwa' (power) as used in this objective. It begins by stating that the intended meaning is the genus of capability (qudra). It then presents the definition given by Ibn Sina: 'the principle of change in another insofar as it is another.' The definition is explained with the example of a physician who treats himself — he acts insofar as he is a physician, and he is acted upon insofar as he is a patient. The commentator then notes that this discussion is occasioned by the need to clarify the different meanings of 'quwwa'. He mentions that other meanings (such as possibility, capability, and passivity) are treated elsewhere, and their mention here is digressive to show the usages of the term. The passage then delves into a mathematical illustration of 'quwwa' in geometry, where the square of the hypotenuse is described as a 'quwwa' for the hypotenuse. This demonstrates the technical usage of the term in mathematics. The commentator explains the concept of square as both a number and a geometric figure, and demonstrates the Pythagorean theorem by numerical example. This illustrates that 'quwwa' can refer to a mathematical power or capacity. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the definition of quwwa applies only to active power, excluding cases where the same entity is acted upon.
Correction: The definition explicitly includes the self-treating physician, showing that quwwa can involve both acting and being acted upon, as long as the change occurs in another (even if that 'another' is the same individual under a different aspect).

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what is the definition of power (quwwa)?
A) The principle of change in itself
B) The principle of change in another insofar as it is another
C) The ability to do anything
D) The genus of possibility

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is defined as the principle of change in another, and this definition intentionally includes cases where the agent and patient are the same entity under different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you strive to change a personal flaw, do you realize that you are both the agent and the recipient of that change? How does acknowledging that your 'quwwa' involves both acting and being acted upon transform your approach to self-reform? Commit today to consciously view your efforts as a single process of guided action and humble receptivity.
?
Think of a time you tried to change but felt you were only acting without allowing yourself to be acted upon. How did that imbalance affect the outcome? What would it look like to embrace the reality that change requires you to be both the physician and the patient? Name one specific step you can take this week to let divine guidance shape your capability.
?
The definition of quwwa includes the principle of change in another, but the self-treating physician shows you can be both. Do you ever separate your spiritual growth into what you do and what God does? How can you unify them into one principle of change in your life? Write down one action you will take today that reflects both your effort and your openness to being transformed.

Mathematical Usage of Quwwa in Geometry
Illustration of the term quwwa as applied to the square of the hypotenuse in geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term quwwa is used in geometry to denote the square of a side as a 'power' for the hypotenuse, and how the Pythagorean theorem illustrates this usage.

Core Idea
In geometry, the square of a side is called a 'quwwa' for the hypotenuse, showing the term's extension beyond physical power.

This lesson delves into a mathematical digression where the commentator explains the usage of quwwa in the context of the Pythagorean theorem. The passage clarifies that the square of a line can be called a 'quwwa' for that line, and illustrates this with both numerical and geometric interpretations of squaring.

Guided Path
1
Read the mathematical passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the relationship between the sides of a right triangle and how the term quwwa is applied to the square of the hypotenuse.
This is a technical illustration; read slowly.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'square'
The commentator explains that 'square' can refer to the product of a number multiplied by itself (numerical square) or to a geometric figure with four equal sides. Both meanings are used in the theorem.
3
Follow the numerical example
The passage provides a numerical example with sides of length 10, showing that the square of the hypotenuse is 200, and thus the hypotenuse is the root of 200, which lies between 14 and 15.
This demonstrates the equality of squares in a concrete way.

Quwwa as Genus of Qudra and Its Four Types
Defines quwwa as genus of qudra and divides it into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of quwwa as the genus of qudra and its classification into four types based on two criteria: number of actions and presence of consciousness.

Core Idea
Quwwa is potential for action, divided by consciousness and whether it produces one or many acts.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif defines quwwa (power) as the genus of qudra (capability). It clarifies that the true agent is the rational soul while the body is affected, and then classifies quwwa into four types. Understanding this classification is essential for Islamic philosophical theology, as it applies to celestial souls, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: definition, clarification, then division.
The passage begins with a specific context about man and medicine, but the main point is the classification.
2
Identify the two criteria for classification
Look for the sentence that introduces the division. It says 'because what issues from quwwa is either one act or different acts, and in both cases either it has consciousness or not.' These are the two criteria: number of actions and consciousness.
3
List the four types and their criteria
Using the two criteria, map each type: celestial soul (one act, with consciousness), elemental nature (one act, without consciousness), animal power (multiple acts, with consciousness), vegetative soul (multiple acts, without consciousness).
Notice that the passage calls the celestial soul 'al-nafs al-falakiyya' and animal power 'al-quwwa al-hayawaniyya'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الإنسان هو هذا النوع الرابع - المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة من حيث هو جسم ينفعل عما يلاقيه من الدواء، وهذا مبني على ما يتبادر إلى الجسم، والتحقيق أن المعالج المؤثر هو النفس الناطقة والمعالج المتأثر هو البدن وهما متغايران بالذات فالأولى أن يمثل بمعالجة الإنسان نفسه في إزالة الأخلاق الرديئة التي هي أمراض نفسانية، وإنما كان هذا القيد موجباً لعموم الحدود ودخول ما كان خارجاً عنه لأن المتبادر من لفظ الآخر هو المغاير بالذات فلما قيد بالحيثية علم أن التغاير بالاعتبار كاف والقوة بهذا المعنى تنقسم إلى أقسام أربعة لأن الصادر من القوة إما فعل واحد، أو أفعال مختلفة، وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون لها شعور بما يصدر عنها أو لا، فالأول : النفس الفلكية والثاني: الطبيعة العنصرية وما في معناها والثالث : القوة الحيوانية والرابع: النفس النباتية، وقد مرت
That man is this fourth type - the thirteenth objective: al-quwwa is the genus of qudra in terms of him being a body that is affected by what encounters it of medicine, and this is based on what occurs to the body, and the verification is that the effective agent is the rational soul and the affected patient is the body, and they are distinct in essence, so it is more appropriate to exemplify by man's treatment of himself in removing bad morals which are psychological diseases. And this qualification necessitated the generality of the definition and the inclusion of what was outside it because what is immediately understood from the word 'other' is that which is distinct in essence, but when it was qualified by 'by virtue of', it became known that distinction by consideration is sufficient. And al-quwwa in this sense is divided into four divisions because what issues from al-quwwa is either one act or different acts, and in both cases either it has consciousness of what issues from it or not. So the first: the celestial soul; the second: elemental nature and what is in its meaning; the third: animal power; the fourth: the vegetative soul, and they have been mentioned.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines al-quwwa as the genus of qudra and divides it into four types. It begins by explaining that man, as a body, is acted upon by medicine, but the true agent is the rational soul, while the body is the patient. The qualification 'by virtue of' broadens the definition because the term 'other' normally implies essential distinction, but here relational distinction suffices. Then it divides quwwa into four: (1) celestial soul (one action, with consciousness), (2) elemental nature (one action, without consciousness), (3) animal power (multiple actions, with consciousness), (4) vegetative soul (multiple actions, without consciousness). This classification is based on two criteria: whether the power produces one or many actions, and whether it has awareness of its action.

طبيعة عنصرية (elemental nature)
قوة حيوانية (animal power)
نفس نباتية (vegetative soul)
الحيثية (by virtue of, relational distinction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of quwwa is purely physical, involving only bodies acting on each other.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the effective agent is the rational soul, and the qualification 'by virtue of' shows that relational distinction is sufficient, not essential distinction.

Checkpoint

A) Number of actions and presence of consciousness
B) Material vs. immaterial, and eternal vs. temporal
C) Essential vs. accidental, and agent vs. patient
D) One act vs. many acts, and rational vs. irrational

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is classified into four types based on whether its action is single or multiple and whether it has consciousness of its action.

You are a human, granted the power of many acts with consciousness. Yet how often do you live like a vegetative soul—growing and moving but without awareness of why? What one step can you take this week to act from your rational soul instead?

The Broadening Qualification and the True Agent in Treatment
Explains the broadening qualification and identifies the true agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a qualification in a definition can broaden rather than restrict, and why the soul is considered the true agent in treatment, with bodily instruments being mere tools.

Core Idea
Qualifications can generalize, and the soul alone is the true agent.

This passage from the commentary addresses two key issues: first, how a qualification that appears to restrict actually broadens the definition by reinterpreting a key term; second, why the rational soul is the true agent in treatment, even though bodily instruments are used. These clarifications are essential for understanding the kalam approach to definitions and agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the repeated structure of objection and answer.
2
Identify the first objection and its answer
Look for the objection that a qualification should exclude what was previously included. The answer distinguishes between specification and interpretation.
The key phrase is 'if it is an interpretation from one meaning to a more general meaning, then it is generalization.'
3
Identify the second objection and its answer
Find the objection that treatment involves bodily instruments, so the agent seems composite. The answer states that instruments are not real agents; only the soul is.
The commentator says 'the instruments of the act are not attributed to the act in reality; rather it is attributed to the agent.'
4
Reflect on the final remark about the qualification
The passage ends with a note that the qualification 'by virtue of' is not a real qualification but removes a mistaken one, sufficiency of relational distinction.
This ties back to the first point about broadening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو النفس الناطقة) وإن كان باعتبار تعلقه بالبدن إذ لا بد في العلاج من استعمال الأعضاء. قوله : ( هو البدن لأنه من حيث كونه متعلق النفس، إذ لا بد من الحياة حين العلاج. قوله : ( وإنما كان إلخ ) دفع لما يتراءى من أنه مخالف لما تقرر من أن القيد في الإثبات للإخراج وحاصل الدفع أنه فيما إذا كان القيد تخصيصاً للإثبات السابق، أما إذا كان تفسيراً له من معنى إلى معنى أعم فهو التعميم. قوله : والتحقيق أن المعالج إلخ) قيل : المعالجة هي الطلب العملي، فهي لا تختص بالنفس لأنها لا يكون إلا بآلات بدنية نعم يمكن ادعاء اختصاص الطب العملي بالنفس وإن كان حصوله لها بمعاونة الآلات البدنية فالمعالج بكسر اللام ليس في التحقيق إلا المجموع، وأما المعالج بالفتح فيحتمل أن يكون هو المجموع أيضاً، لأن النفس لما جاز تأثرها عما ارتسم في قواها بأن ينتقش بكليات تلك الجزئيات المرتسمة في القوى، ويحصل لها بواسطة تلك الانتقاش أعراض نفسانية كالغضب ونحوه جاز أن يتأثر أيضاً من الأحوال البدنية العارضة للقوى من الصحة والمرض والجواب أن آلات الفعل لا يسند إليها الفعل حقيقة وإنما يسند إلى الفاعل. قوله : ( وإنما كان هذا القيد موجباً إلخ إشارة إلى جواب ما يقال : المعقول كون القيد في الإثبات موجباً لخصوص الحد وخروج ما كان داخلاً قبله فكيف انعكس الأمر هاهنا، وحاصل الجواب أن المذكور هاهنا ليس بقيد لما قبله من التحقيق، بل إزالة للقيد المتوهم منه، وهو وجوب المغايرة الذاتية لكن في دلالة قيد الحيثية على كفاية التغاير الاعتباري نوع مناقشة يندفع بلزوم استدراكها وإذا لم يحمل عليه فتأمل
His saying: ('He is the rational soul') even if it is with consideration of its relation to the body, since treatment necessarily involves the use of limbs. His saying: ('He is the body') because in terms of it being related to the soul, since life is necessarily present during treatment. His saying: ('And this qualification' etc.) is a rebuttal of what appears to be contradictory to what is established that a qualification in affirmation is for exclusion, and the gist of the rebuttal is that it applies when the qualification is a specification of the previous affirmation, but if it is an interpretation from one meaning to a more general meaning, then it is generalization. His saying: ('And the verification is that the agent' etc.) It was said: al-mu'ālaja (treatment) is practical seeking, so it is not specific to the soul because it only occurs with bodily instruments. Yes, it can be claimed that practical medicine is specific to the soul even if its attainment is with the assistance of bodily instruments. So the agent (mu'ālij with kasr of lām) in reality is nothing but the composite. As for the patient (mu'ālaj with fatḥ of lām), it is possible that it is also the composite, because when the soul is susceptible to being affected by what is imprinted in its powers such that it is impressed by the universals of those particulars imprinted in the powers, and by means of that impression psychological accidents like anger and the like occur to it, it is possible that it also be affected by the physical states that occur to the powers from health and disease. And the answer is that the instruments of the act are not attributed to the act in reality; rather it is attributed to the agent. His saying: ('And this qualification necessitated' etc.) is an indication of the answer to what is said: It is reasonable that a qualification in affirmation necessitates specificity of the definition and exclusion of what was previously included, so how is the matter reversed here? The gist of the answer is that what is mentioned here is not a qualification of what preceded it of verification, rather it is removing the misunderstanding of a qualification, which is the necessity of essential distinction. But there is some contention in the indication of the qualification 'by virtue of' for the sufficiency of relational distinction, which is repelled by the necessity of its completion. And if it is not taken thus, then reflect.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that addresses objections to the earlier definition. It explains that the qualification 'by virtue of' does not restrict but expands the definition, because it interprets the term 'other' in a broader sense, making relational difference sufficient. It also discusses the true agent: although the body and soul both seem to be involved, the act is truly attributed to the soul alone, as instruments are not real agents. Finally, it answers the objection that a qualification should specify: here it removes a mistaken qualification (that essential distinction is needed), and the sufficiency of relational distinction is accepted.

مغايرة بالاعتبار (relational distinction)
معالج (agent)
آلات (instruments)
فاعل (agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qualifications always restrict definitions, and the composite of soul and body is the true agent in treatment.
Correction: Qualifications can generalize when they reinterpret terms, and the soul alone is the true agent; bodily instruments are not agents.

Checkpoint

A) Because it specifies that the distinction must be essential
B) Because it interprets 'other' to mean merely relational difference
C) Because it limits the definition to the soul only
D) Because it removes the need for any distinction

Key Takeaway
Qualifications can generalize by reinterpreting terms, and the soul alone is the true agent; instruments are not agents.

Quwwa as a Common Accident
Al-Razi's argument that quwwa is a common accident, not a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why quwwa (power) is classified as a common accident rather than a genus.

Core Idea
Quwwa cannot be a genus because it spans both substances and accidents.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi argues that quwwa, which includes both substantial and accidental forms, cannot be a genus. Instead, it is a common accident. This distinction is foundational for understanding how kalam categorizes metaphysical concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the key terms.
2
Understand the argument
The argument is that quwwa includes both substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad). Because a true genus cannot apply to two different categories, quwwa must be a common accident.
A common accident is a property shared across categories.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: it affects how we classify and reason about divine attributes and created powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Reference to them. Imam al-Razi said: Some of these divisions are substantial forms and some are accidental forms, so al-quwwa is not predicated of them as a genus, but as a common accident, due to the impossibility of substances and accidents sharing a generic description. And al-quwwa is also said for imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l (possibility opposite to actuality) because this imkan is a cause of qudra over it, i.e., over the thing to which this imkan attaches, metaphorically. And that is because qudra only has effect in accordance with volition which must be concomitant with the non-existence of the desired object. So if not for the imkan concomitant with non-existence, which is called the act, qudra would not have effect on that desired object. So this imkan is a cause of qudra apparently. And since qudra is named quwwa, its name was applied to its cause. And he did not make imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l a necessary concomitant of qudra as Imam al-Razi claimed, and its justification is that al-qadir (the capable) is he from whom either action or omission is sound, as we have transmitted. Because the necessary concomitant of qudra upon its direction is imkan al-dhati (essential possibility), not imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l. And to draw attention to this, the author said: 'And this' i.e., imkan al-muqabil named quwwa, 'is other than imkan al-dhati, for it' i.e., imkan al-dhati, 'may accompany the act', for the actually black thing, its blackness is possible essentially, 'and it is reflected from both sides', i.e., the two sides of existence and non-existence: that which is possible of existence is also possible of non-existence and vice versa, unlike 'this imkan al-muqabil', for it cannot be conceived to accompany the act. His saying: ('in a generic description') otherwise the ten categories would not be highest genera. His saying: ('metaphorically') is attached to his saying: 'cause', for it is one of the things on which qudra depends, so it was its cause. His saying: ('and what is in its meaning') like the specific forms of compounds, such as the cooling form of opium and the heating form of euphorbium, and like heat and cold as has passed. His saying: ('metaphorically') is attached in meaning to his saying: 'cause of qudra over it.' It cannot be said: this is indicated by its delay after that statement, and also the statement of the commentator indicates: 'so this imkan is a cause of qudra apparently.' His saying: ('which must be concomitant with the non-existence of the desired object') it has preceded that volition must be concomitant with the desired object according to the people of verification, so this statement is for others, perhaps the philosophers. His saying: ('because the necessary concomitant of qudra upon its direction is imkan al-dhati') there is discussion in it, since it is possible that al-Imam meant the possibility of the act from the agent as is apparent, and it will come in the discussions of takwin from the divine sciences that validity from a person is other than imkan al-dhati which is validity in itself. For if you said: Interpreting imkan as what I mentioned is not plausible, because his intent is to explain the relationship for applying quwwa to imkan, and the imkan to which quwwa is applied is not that imkan in the sense you mentioned. I say: This objection applies to both meanings, since the imkan to which quwwa is applied is also not imkan al-dhati, but rather isti'dadi (preparatory possibility). And the answer, assuming the equality of the two imkans in the non-application of quwwa to them, is that the restriction in his statement 'it is imkan al-dhati' is relative, indicated by his saying 'not imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l', so reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage addresses the metaphysical status of quwwa. Imam al-Razi argues that quwwa cannot be a genus because its divisions include both substances and accidents, and a genus cannot span both categories; thus quwwa is a common accident. Then it discusses the metaphorical application of 'quwwa' to imkan (possibility) insofar as imkan is a cause of qudra. A distinction is made between two types of imkan: imkan dhati (essential possibility) which can coexist with actuality, and imkan muqabil lil-fi'l (possibility opposite to actuality) which cannot. The latter is the one called quwwa metaphorically. Further commentary clarifies that the necessary concomitant of qudra is imkan dhati, not imkan muqabil, and that the imkan that is a cause is preparatory possibility (isti'dadi). The passage also addresses potential objections about the meaning of imkan and its relation to qudra.

جنس
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quwwa is a true genus like other categories.
Correction: Quwwa cannot be a genus because it includes both substances and accidents, which are distinct categories.

A) Because it can cause things
B) Because it applies to both substances and accidents

D) Because it is a common accident

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is a common accident, not a genus, because it applies to both substances and accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How would distinguishing between essence and accident change your self-accountability?
?
Al-Razi's argument rests on the principle that substances and accidents cannot share a genus. This is a logical boundary. Do you respect boundaries when it comes to Allah? In your heart, have you ever allowed a created concept to shape your understanding of the Creator in a way that implies sharing a genus? Read the Arabic again: 'امتناع اشتراك الجواهر والأعراض في وصف جنسي'. Let that impossibility compel you to purify your tawheed.

Metaphorical Use of Quwwa and Distinction of Imkans
Explains the metaphorical use of quwwa for imkan and distinguishes two types of imkan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how 'quwwa' is used metaphorically for imkan muqabil lil-fi'l and how it differs from imkan dhati.

Core Idea
Quwwa is figuratively applied to imkan muqabil, which is a cause of qudra, but imkan dhati is the true concomitant of qudra.

Here the author explains the metaphorical relationship between quwwa and imkan. He distinguishes two types of possibility: imkan dhati (essential possibility) which can coexist with actuality, and imkan muqabil lil-fi'l (possibility opposite to actuality) which cannot. The latter is called quwwa metaphorically because it is a cause of qudra.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the two types of imkan mentioned.
2
Understand the metaphorical relation
Quwwa is literally qudra (power). It is applied metaphorically to imkan muqabil because this imkan is a cause of qudra. The passage explains the causality: qudra acts through volition, and imkan muqabil is necessary for that effect.
Metaphor is in the phrase 'سبب للقدرة'—cause of qudra.
3
Distinguish the two imkans
Imkan dhati can coexist with the act (e.g., a black thing is essentially possible even when actual). Imkan muqabil cannot coexist with the act; it is opposite to actuality. This distinction is crucial for understanding qudra's relationship to possibility.

Reference to them. Imam al-Razi said: Some of these divisions are substantial forms and some are accidental forms, so al-quwwa is not predicated of them as a genus, but as a common accident, due to the impossibility of substances and accidents sharing a generic description. And al-quwwa is also said for imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l (possibility opposite to actuality) because this imkan is a cause of qudra over it, i.e., over the thing to which this imkan attaches, metaphorically. And that is because qudra only has effect in accordance with volition which must be concomitant with the non-existence of the desired object. So if not for the imkan concomitant with non-existence, which is called the act, qudra would not have effect on that desired object. So this imkan is a cause of qudra apparently. And since qudra is named quwwa, its name was applied to its cause. And he did not make imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l a necessary concomitant of qudra as Imam al-Razi claimed, and its justification is that al-qadir (the capable) is he from whom either action or omission is sound, as we have transmitted. Because the necessary concomitant of qudra upon its direction is imkan al-dhati (essential possibility), not imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l. And to draw attention to this, the author said: 'And this' i.e., imkan al-muqabil named quwwa, 'is other than imkan al-dhati, for it' i.e., imkan al-dhati, 'may accompany the act', for the actually black thing, its blackness is possible essentially, 'and it is reflected from both sides', i.e., the two sides of existence and non-existence: that which is possible of existence is also possible of non-existence and vice versa, unlike 'this imkan al-muqabil', for it cannot be conceived to accompany the act. His saying: ('and what is in its meaning') like the specific forms of compounds, such as the cooling form of opium and the heating form of euphorbium, and like heat and cold as has passed. His saying: ('metaphorically') is attached in meaning to his saying: 'cause of qudra over it.' It cannot be said: this is indicated by its delay after that statement, and also the statement of the commentator indicates: 'so this imkan is a cause of qudra apparently.' His saying: ('which must be concomitant with the non-existence of the desired object') it has preceded that volition must be concomitant with the desired object according to the people of verification, so this statement is for others, perhaps the philosophers. His saying: ('because the necessary concomitant of qudra upon its direction is imkan al-dhati') there is discussion in it, since it is possible that al-Imam meant the possibility of the act from the agent as is apparent, and it will come in the discussions of takwin from the divine sciences that validity from a person is other than imkan al-dhati which is validity in itself. For if you said: Interpreting imkan as what I mentioned is not plausible, because his intent is to explain the relationship for applying quwwa to imkan, and the imkan to which quwwa is applied is not that imkan in the sense you mentioned. I say: This objection applies to both meanings, since the imkan to which quwwa is applied is also not imkan al-dhati, but rather isti'dadi (preparatory possibility). And the answer, assuming the equality of the two imkans in the non-application of quwwa to them, is that the restriction in his statement 'it is imkan al-dhati' is relative, indicated by his saying 'not imkan al-muqabil lil-fi'l', so reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage addresses the metaphysical status of quwwa. Imam al-Razi argues that quwwa cannot be a genus because its divisions include both substances and accidents, and a genus cannot span both categories; thus quwwa is a common accident. Then it discusses the metaphorical application of 'quwwa' to imkan (possibility) insofar as imkan is a cause of qudra. A distinction is made between two types of imkan: imkan dhati (essential possibility) which can coexist with actuality, and imkan muqabil lil-fi'l (possibility opposite to actuality) which cannot. The latter is the one called quwwa metaphorically. Further commentary clarifies that the necessary concomitant of qudra is imkan dhati, not imkan muqabil, and that the imkan that is a cause is preparatory possibility (isti'dadi). The passage also addresses potential objections about the meaning of imkan and its relation to qudra.

Correction: They are distinct: imkan dhati can coexist with actuality, while imkan muqabil cannot.

?

The Multiple Meanings of Quwwa (Power) in Kalam
The author defines quwwa as the genus of qudra and distinguishes its meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of quwwa (power) as the genus of capability, including its usage for possibility opposed to actuality, for capability itself, and for capacity for arduous actions, and the distinction between essential possibility and possibility opposed to actuality.

Core Idea
Quwwa has multiple meanings: genus, possibility, capability, and capacity—each with a distinct logical role.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explores the term 'quwwa' (power) and its relationship to 'qudra' (capability). The author begins by stating that quwwa is the genus of qudra, but immediately clarifies that when predicated of different forms, it is a common accident rather than a true genus. He then distinguishes several meanings: possibility opposed to actuality (which is metaphorically called a cause of capability), capability itself, and the capacity for arduous actions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the nuanced terminology of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a definition and then proceeds to critiques and distinctions.
2
Identify the multiple meanings of quwwa
As you read, list the distinct meanings the author gives for 'quwwa': genus (with qualification), possibility opposed to actuality, capability itself, and capacity for arduous actions.
These meanings are not all used in the same way; some are technical, some are common.
3
Understand the critique of Imam al-Razi
The author disagrees with al-Razi's claim that the possibility opposed to actuality is a necessary concomitant of capability. Follow the reasoning: capability requires essential possibility, not opposed possibility.
Essential possibility is convertible and can coexist with actuality; opposed possibility cannot.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The hypothetical objector says that if essential possibility is restricted to non-existence, it becomes the opposed possibility. The author responds with examples (blackness, air, sperm-drop) showing they are not always identical.
This shows that essential possibility is broader than the opposed possibility.
5
Reflect on the warning about capacity
The final meaning—capacity for arduous actions—might be mistaken as a cause of capability, but the author clarifies that capability is actually the principle of this power.
This prevents a misunderstanding about causal relations.

( وقد تقال ) القوة ( في العرف للقدرة نفسها وهذا تكرار لما ذكره أولا ( و ) تقال : القوة ( لما به القدرة على الأفعال الشاقة ) وهذه العبارة توهم أن القوة بهذا المعنى سبب للقدرة ومبدأ لها وليس كذلك، بل الأمر بالعكس، القدرة مبدأ لهذه القوة ففي المباحث المشرقية أن القوة
The fourth type - the thirteenth objective: Power (al-quwwa) is the genus of capability (al-qudra). Reference to it: Imam al-Razi said: Some of these divisions are substantial forms and some are accidental forms, so power is not predicated of them as a genus, but rather as a common accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), due to the impossibility of substances and accidents sharing a generic description. And 'power' is said for possibility opposed to actuality (al-imkān al-muqābil lil-fiʿl) because this possibility is a cause of capability for it, i.e., for the thing to which this possibility pertains, metaphorically. That is because capability only influences in accordance with the will that must be concomitant with the non-existence of the intended object; were it not for the possibility concomitant with non-existence, which is called actuality, capability would not influence that intended object. So this possibility is a cause of capability apparently. And since capability is named 'power', its name was applied to its cause. And he did not make the possibility opposed to actuality necessary concomitant of capability as Imam al-Razi claimed, and its justification is that the capable one is he from whom act or omission is possible, as we have transmitted. Because the necessary concomitant for capability in its direction is essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī), not the one opposed to actuality. And to indicate that, the author said: 'And this'—i.e., the opposed possibility named power—'is other than essential possibility; for it'—i.e., essential possibility—'may accompany actuality: for blackness actually can have its blackness essentially, and it is convertible from both sides (i.e., the two sides of existence and non-existence: for what is possible of existence is also possible of non-existence, and vice versa, unlike this opposed possibility, for its concurrency with actuality is inconceivable, nor is it convertible, since the existence of blackness and its non-existence cannot be together potentially.' Then if you say: It is known from what you mentioned that essential possibility, when restricted by concurrency with non-existence, becomes opposed to actuality and named 'power.' I say: It may be so, as in the example of blackness, and it may not be, for air may become water by this possibility without essential possibility, and the sperm-drop may become a human while our statement 'Nothing of the sperm-drop is a human' is necessarily true. So reflect. And 'power' may be said in common usage for capability itself, and this is repetition of what he mentioned first. And it is said: 'power' for that by which there is capability for arduous actions. This expression gives the impression that power in this sense is a cause of capability and a principle for it, but it is not so; rather the opposite is true: capability is a principle for this power. For in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya that power...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'quwwa' (power) in relation to 'qudra' (capability) and distinguishes its various meanings. The author begins by stating that quwwa is the genus (jins) of capability, meaning that capability falls under the broader category of power. He then references Imam al-Razi, who notes that the different forms (some substantial, some accidental) cannot share a genuine genus; rather, 'quwwa' is predicated of them as a common accident, because substances and accidents cannot share a generic description. Next, the author explains one meaning of 'quwwa': it is used for the possibility opposed to actuality (al-imkān al-muqābil lil-fiʿl). This possibility is metaphorically called a cause of capability because capability acts in accordance with will, which must accompany the non-existence of the intended act. Without this possibility of non-existence, capability could not bring about the act. Thus, this possibility is considered a cause of capability in appearance. Since capability is named 'quwwa', the name of the effect is applied to its cause. The author then critiques Imam al-Razi's claim that this opposed possibility is a necessary concomitant of capability. Instead, the author argues that the necessary concomitant for capability is essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī), not the opposed possibility. To clarify, the author quotes the original text (al-Mawaqif) stating that the opposed possibility is different from essential possibility: essential possibility can coexist with actuality (e.g., blackness that actually exists can still be possible essentially), and it is convertible (if something is possible in existence, it is possible in non-existence and vice versa). In contrast, the opposed possibility cannot coexist with actuality and is not convertible. A hypothetical objection is then raised: if essential possibility is restricted to concurrency with non-existence, it becomes identical to the opposed possibility and could be called 'quwwa'. The author responds by giving examples: for blackness, the restricted essential possibility does coincide with the opposed possibility, but for air becoming water or a sperm-drop becoming a human, the essential possibility (in the sense of intrinsic possibility) does not hold; only the preparatory possibility (al-imkān al-istiʿdādī) is present. Thus, the two are not always the same. Finally, the author discusses two other common usages of 'quwwa': (1) in common parlance, 'quwwa' may be used synonymously with 'qudra' (capability itself)—this is deemed a repetition of an earlier point; (2) 'quwwa' may refer to the capacity for arduous actions. The author warns that this second usage might mislead one into thinking that such 'quwwa' is a cause of capability, but in reality, capability is the principle of this power, as stated in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that quwwa (power) is identical to qudra (capability) in all contexts, or that the possibility opposed to actuality is a necessary concomitant of capability.
Correction: The passage shows that quwwa has multiple meanings—including genus, possibility, capability, and capacity—and that the necessary concomitant of capability is essential possibility, not the opposed possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between al-imkān al-dhātī (essential possibility) and al-imkān al-muqābil lil-fiʿl (possibility opposed to actuality)?
A) They are identical in all cases.
B) Essential possibility can coexist with actuality and is convertible, while the opposed possibility cannot.
C) The opposed possibility is a type of essential possibility.
D) They are both necessary concomitants of qudra.

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is not a univocal term; it has distinct meanings in kalam, and confusing essential possibility with possibility opposed to actuality leads to errors in reasoning about capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that you often mistake your own potential capacity (imkān muqābil lil-fiʿl) for true capability (qudra). Think of one area in your life—worship, relationships, or personal change—where you have used 'it's impossible for me' as an excuse. Is that impossibility real or just a lack of will? Will you now act based on the essential possibility that Allah has given you, regardless of your current state?
?
The author argues that the possibility opposed to actuality cannot accompany actuality. In your own heart, do you hold onto the possibility of sin even while claiming to be a believer? Are there habits or thoughts that you keep as 'possible' when they should be dead to you? Commit to one specific thing you will treat as non-existent by God's grace.
?
Imam al-Razi's error was to assume a certain condition (the opposed possibility) was necessary for capability. Where have you assumed that a certain feeling, circumstance, or past failure is necessary before you can obey God or seek knowledge? Will you today act from your essential capacity, trusting that Allah's command is sufficient? What is one step you will take within the next 24 hours to prove that your capability is not dependent on that condition?

Force as the Genus of Power and Two Types of Potentiality
Definition of al-quwwa and two types of potentiality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-quwwa as the genus of al-qudra and the distinction between essential potentiality and dispositional potentiality.

Core Idea
Force is the genus of power; potentiality has two distinct forms.

This passage from the Thirteenth Objective establishes the foundational concept of force (al-quwwa) as the broader category under which power (al-qudra) falls. It then distinguishes two kinds of potentiality—essential and dispositional—using concrete examples like black becoming white and air becoming water. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions on divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-quwwa
Start by reading the passage stating that force is the genus of power. Note the initial statement and the reversal mentioned.
The 'reversal' refers to the impossibility of a thing and its opposite being in potentiality simultaneously.
2
Examine the example of blackness
The author uses the example of blackness to illustrate essential potentiality. A white object can become black without changing its essence.
3
Contrast with air becoming water
The example of air becoming water shows dispositional potentiality, which requires a change in the thing's specific form.
This highlights the key difference: essential potentiality does not alter the substance, while dispositional does.

قوله : ( مما ذكرت إلخ) من قوله : فلولا الإمكان المقارن للعدم وهو الذي يقال الفعل إلخ . قوله : ( كما في مثال السواد من قولنا للأبيض : الأسود بالقوة فإنه يستعد السواد ويمكن لذاته البياض بناء على أنه لا يتغير حينئذ، فإنه عند حصول البياض . قوله : ( فإن الهواء إلخ) فإنه مستعد لأن يصير هواء بزوال صورته النوعية، وحصول الصورة المائية وليس ممكناً له بالنظر إلى ذاته لامتناع اجتماع ،الصورتين والحاصل: إن الإمكان الاستعدادي مع التغير في ذات المستعد بخلاف الإمكان الذاتي. قوله : ( وهذا تكرار (إلخ إلا أن المقصود من ذكره أولاً بيان وجه المناسبة لإيراد معاني القوة في مباحث القدرة ومن ذكره هاهنا بيان إطلاق القوة عليها .
قوله : ( هذه العبارة (توهم فالأولى أن يقال للقدرة على الأفعال الشاقة.
قوله : (قد يكون الأمر كذلك كما في مثال السواد إلخ) حاصل الفرق بين المثالين أن الأسود من غير تغير في ذاته يمكن أن يصير أبيض وأما الهواء فإنما يصير ماء إذا غير صورته النوعية الداخلة في قوامه فلا يمكن للهواء المركب من الهيولى والصورة المخصوصة إمكاناً ذاتياً أن يصير ماء، نعم لو أريد الهواء هيولاه مثلاً لأمكن ذلك إمكاناً ذاتياً إن قلت: فهذا المجموع لا يمكن إمكاناً استعدادياً أن يصير ماء فلا فرق . قلت : الاستعداد إنما يعتبر بالقياس إلى المادة وإن وصف به المجموع ظاهراً ولا كذلك الإمكان الذاتي ..
قوله : ( وهذا تكرار لما ذكره (أولاً فيه بحث لأن التكرار إنما يلزم على ما حمل نفسه كلام المصنف عليه، وأما لو حمل قوله أولا والمراد هاهنا جنسه أن المراد في هذا المحل بالقدرة التي أطاق عليها القوة جنسها وحمل قوله ثانيا وقد يقال : في العرف للقدرة نفسها أنها قد يطلق على نفس القدرة لم يكن تكراراً أصلاً، فإن قلت قوله في العرف يأبى عن حمل القدرة في كلامه الثاني على تمامها إذ إطلاق القوة في العرف على جنس القدرة ليس إلا . قلت لو سلم لا تكرار حينئذ أيضاً لأنا نحمل القدرة في كلامه السابق على تمامها لا على جنسها وفي الثاني على جنسها، فتأمل. قوله : ( ففي المباحث المشرقية تعليل لقوله : بل الأمر بالعكس بناء على أن المفهوم من كلامها متبوعية القدرة للقوة .
The Fourth Type - The Thirteenth Objective: The force (al-quwwa) is the genus of power (al-qudra). It is reversed, for it is impossible for the existence and non-existence of blackness together to be in potentiality. If you say: It has become known from what I mentioned that essential potentiality (al-imkan al-dhati) when bound by lack of existence (muqaranat al-`adam) is opposite to actuality and called potentiality (al-quwwa). I say: It may be so as in the example of blackness, and it may not be. For air can become water by this potentiality without essential potentiality, and the sperm can become a human while our statement is true: Nothing of the sperm is a human by necessity. So reflect. (And it is sometimes said) force (al-quwwa) in custom for power (al-qudra) itself, and this is a repetition of what he mentioned earlier. (And) it is said: force (al-quwwa) for that by which there is power over difficult actions. This expression suggests that force in this sense is a cause of power and a principle for it, but it is not so; rather the opposite, power is a principle for this force. For in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that force... His saying: (together in potentiality because it would entail the raising of the two opposites and their conjunction. His saying: (from what I mentioned etc.) from his saying: Were it not for the potentiality bound by lack of existence which is called actuality etc. His saying: (as in the example of blackness) from our saying concerning the white: black in potentiality, for it is disposed to blackness and it is possible in its essence for whiteness on the basis that it does not change then, for when whiteness occurs... His saying: (for air etc.) for it is disposed to become air by the removal of its specific form and the acquisition of the watery form, and it is not possible for it with respect to its essence due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the two forms. The summary: that dispositional potentiality (al-imkan al-isti`dadi) involves change in the essence of the disposed thing, unlike essential potentiality. His saying: (and this is repetition etc.) except that the purpose of mentioning it first is to clarify the aspect of suitability for introducing the meanings of force in the investigations of power, and its mention here is to clarify the application of force to it. His saying: (this expression suggests) so it is better to say: the capacity for difficult actions. His saying: (it may be so as in the example of blackness etc.) The summary of the difference between the two examples is that what is black, without change in its essence, can become white, while air only becomes water if it changes its specific form entering into its constitution. So it is not possible for air composed of matter and specific form by essential potentiality to become water. Yes, if by air one means its matter, for example, then that would be possible by essential potentiality. If you say: This composite cannot by dispositional potentiality become water, so there is no difference. I say: Disposition is only considered in relation to matter, even if the composite is described by it in appearance, and this is not the case for essential potentiality. His saying: (And this is a repetition of what he mentioned earlier) contains discussion, for repetition would only follow based on his interpretation of the author's words. But if one interprets his earlier statement as meaning here 'its genus' so that what is meant in this place by power upon which force is exerted is its genus, and interprets his second statement 'and it is said in custom for power itself' as that it may be applied to power itself, then there is no repetition at all. If you say: His statement 'in custom' prevents interpreting his second statement as referring to the complete power, since the application of force in custom to the genus of power is nothing else. I say: Even if we concede, there is no repetition then either, because we interpret his earlier statement as referring to the complete power, and the second as referring to its genus. So reflect. His saying: (For in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya) is a justification for his statement: Rather the opposite, based on the understanding from its speech that power is consequent upon force.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'al-quwwa' (force/potentiality) as the genus of 'al-qudra' (power). It begins by stating the objective: force is the genus of power. The author then discusses the reversal of the relationship between potentiality and actuality, using the example of blackness (cannot be both existent and non-existent simultaneously in potentiality). He distinguishes between essential potentiality (al-imkan al-dhati) and dispositional potentiality (al-imkan al-isti`dadi). Essential potentiality is the intrinsic possibility of something to be something else without changing its nature (e.g., black becoming white). Dispositional potentiality involves a change in the essence of the thing (e.g., air becoming water by losing its form). The passage then addresses three conventional meanings of 'quwwa': (1) as a synonym for 'qudra' itself, (2) as the capacity for difficult actions, and (3) as the quality of non-passivity (discussed later). For the second meaning, the author clarifies that this capacity is not the cause of power, but rather an effect of power; power is the principle of this capacity. The commentary (sharh) follows the matn line by line, explaining each point, providing the example of the white object being black in potentiality, and the air being water in dispositional potentiality. It also resolves an apparent repetition in the matn by interpreting the first mention as referring to the complete power and the second to its genus. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya to support the claim that power is consequent upon force, meaning force in the sense of capacity for difficult actions is dependent on power, not the other way around.

القدرة
الإمكان الذاتي
الإمكان الاستعدادي

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between essential potentiality and dispositional potentiality according to the passage?
A) Essential potentiality involves change in the substance, while dispositional does not.
B) Dispositional potentiality requires a change in the thing's specific form, while essential does not.
C) There is no difference; they are synonymous.
D) Essential potentiality is limited to colors.

Key Takeaway
Force is the genus of power, and potentiality is divided into essential (unchanging essence) and dispositional (changing essence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, are you assuming that you can become better (e.g., more patient, more sincere) through essential potentiality—without changing your inner nature? Or are you ready to undergo the dispositional change that actually transforms you? What one specific area are you avoiding because it requires real change?
?
The example of air becoming water shows that some changes require a complete alteration of essence. Is there a habit or sin you are clinging to because you think you can keep your current self and just add goodness? Are you willing to let your 'air' become 'water'—to be made into something new by Allah? What step can you take today to begin that transformation?
?
Force is the genus of power—all ability ultimately comes from Allah. Have you been relying on your own 'essential potentiality' (thinking you can change by yourself) instead of humbly seeking His power to enact real dispositional change? Write a sincere dua asking Him to grant you the strength to change what needs changing.

Three Meanings of Force and the Power Precedence
Enumeration of three meanings of al-quwwa and correction of causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conventional meanings of al-quwwa and the correct causal relationship between force (capacity for difficult actions) and power.

Core Idea
Force is not the cause of power; power is the principle of force.

After defining force as a genus, the text explains three ways the term 'force' is used in customary language: as a synonym for power, as the capacity for difficult actions, and as a quality of non-passivity. Crucially, the passage corrects a potential misunderstanding: the capacity for difficult actions is not the cause of power; instead, power is the source of this capacity. This lesson clarifies these meanings and their interrelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
The passage lists three conventional meanings of 'quwwa': (1) synonym for qudra, (2) capacity for difficult actions, (3) quality of non-passivity (implied but not fully quoted).
The third meaning is discussed later in the text.
2
Understand the repetition issue
The first meaning seems to repeat an earlier point. The commentary resolves this by interpreting the first mention as referring to the complete power and the second to its genus.
3
Grasp the causal relationship
For the second meaning, the author corrects a possible mistake: the capacity for difficult actions is not the cause of power; rather, power is the principle of this capacity. This is supported by reference to al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.

?

Defining Khuluq: Character as a Disposition from the Soul
Definition of al-khuluq with conditions and illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'khuluq' (character) in Islamic theology and the three conditions that distinguish it from other psychic qualities.

Core Idea
Character is a settled soul-disposition from which action flows spontaneously.

In this passage from al-Mawaqif, the author defines the key term 'khuluq' (character/moral disposition). The definition is precise: a psychic quality that is stable (malaka), originating from the soul, and producing actions without deliberation. This lesson unpacks that definition and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition and translation
Start by reading the Arabic matn and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: al-khuluq, al-malaka, al-ru'ya, al-nafs. Pay attention to the examples (writing, playing tambour) and the three conditions listed at the end.
2
Identify the three conditions
From the passage list the three requirements for a psychic quality to be called 'khuluq': (1) it must be a settled disposition (malaka), not a transient state; (2) it must be a principle of action issuing from the soul itself, not from the limbs; (3) the action must be performed easily, without deliberation or difficulty.
The commentary clarifies that bodily skills like writing are excluded by condition (2).
3
Apply the definition to examples
Consider traits like generosity, patience, or anger. For each, assess whether it meets the three conditions. Would it be considered 'khuluq'? Use the passage's criteria to decide.
For instance, a naturally generous person who gives easily has a disposition from the soul – that qualifies as khuluq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الرابع عشر : الخلق ملكة تصدر عنها الأفعال
بهذا المعنى كأنها زيادة وشدة في المعنى الذي هو القدرة، وقد قيل : أراد هنا بالقدرة على الأفعال الشاقة التمكن منها (و) تقال القوة لعدم الانفعال) والقوة بهذا المعنى من الكيفيات الاستعدادية، وهي بمعنى القدرة إذا خصت بالأعراض من الكيفيات النفسانية.
[ المقصد الرابع عشر : الخلق ملكة تصدر عنها الأفعال ]
وفي النسخ المشهورة الرابع عشر الخلق ملكة تصدر عنها أي عن النفس بسببها (الأفعال بلا روية كمن يكتب شيئا من غير أن يروي في حرف حرف، أو يضرب الطنبور من غير أن يفكر في نغمة نغمة ) أو في نقرة ،نقرة فالكيفية النفسانية إذا لم تكن ملكة لا تسمى خلقاً، وإذا كانت ملكة ولم تكن مبدأ لصدور الفعل عن النفس لم تسم أيضاً خلقاً، وإذا كانت مبداً له بعسر وتأمل لم تكن خلقاً، وإذا اجتمعت فيها
قوله : ( زيادة وشدة والمعنى الأول أصل ومبدأ لها
قوله : ( التمكن منها لا مبدأ التمكن حتى يتوهم ما ذكر.
قوله : ( عدم الانفعال أي كون بحيث لا ينفعل ليكون معنى الكيفيات النفسانية. قوله : (إذا خصت بالاعراض أي لم تجعل شاملة لقدرته تعالى .
قوله : (أي عن النفس إلخ) فإسناد الصدور إلى الملكة مجازي باعتبار كونه آلة . قوله : ( بلا روية) في القاموس رويت في الأمر تروية إذا نظرت وفكرت وعقبه بقوله والاسم الروية أي الفكر.
قوله : (كمن يكتب إلخ تنظير لا تمثيل لما صرح به في شرح المقاصد من أن الراسخ الذي يكون مبدأ لأفعال الجوارح بسهولة لا يسمى خلقاً كملكة الكتابة، والمراد بأفعال النفس ما لا يكون بخصوصية جارحة تدخل فيه وبأفعال الجوارح خلافه .
قوله : ( في نعمة إن أريد الفعل الغائي أو نقرة إن أريد الفعل القريب وهو المذكور في الكتب المشهورة .
قوله : (إذا لم تكن ملكة كغضب الحليم .
قوله : ( ولم تكن مبدأ لصدور الفعل عن النفس) بل عن الجوارح كملكة الكتابة .
قوله : ( وإذا كانت إلخ كالبخيل إذا جاد والكريم إن أنفقه يكون تصرفات الهمت على وفق اقتضاء القوة العقلية ليسلم أن يستعبد الهواء ويستخدمها للذات والفجوز الوقوع في ازدياد اللذات على ما ينبغي والخمود السكون عن طلب ما رخص فيه العقل والشرع من مي اللذات
قوله : (من غير أن يفكر في نغمة قال الله رحمه المشهور في الكتب في نقرة نقرة لأنها الفعل الصادر، وإنما النغمة تحصل منها .
The Fourth Type - The Fourteenth Objective: The character (al-khuluq) is a disposition (malaka) from which actions issue. In this sense, it is like an increase and intensity in the meaning which is power. And it has been said: What is meant here by power over difficult actions is the capacity for them. (And) force (al-quwwa) is said for non-passivity (adam al-inf`al), and force in this sense is among the dispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-isti`dadiyya), and it is in the sense of power when restricted to accidents among the psychic qualities. [The Fourteenth Objective: The character is a disposition from which actions issue.] And in the well-known copies: the fourteenth, character is a disposition from which—i.e., from the soul by means of it—(actions issue without deliberation (la bi-ru’ya), like someone who writes something without deliberating over each letter, or plays the tambour without thinking about each note) or about each pluck? For the psychic quality, if it is not a disposition, it is not called character; and if it is a disposition but is not a principle of the action issuing from the soul, it is also not called character; and if it is a principle for it with difficulty and reflection, it is not character; and if they are combined in it... His saying: (increase and intensity, and the first meaning is an origin and principle for it. His saying: (the capacity for them, not the principle of capacity, so that what was mentioned is not mistakenly thought. His saying: (non-passivity, i.e., being such that it is not affected, so that it is a meaning of psychic qualities. His saying: (when restricted to accidents) i.e., not made inclusive of His power, may He be exalted. His saying: (i.e., from the soul etc.) so the attribution of issuing to the disposition is metaphorical considering it as an instrument. His saying: (without deliberation) in al-Qamus: He deliberated (rawa) in the matter, meaning he considered and thought, and he added that the noun is al-ru’ya, i.e., thought. His saying: (like someone who writes etc.) is an analogy, not an example, as is stated in Sharh al-Maqasid that the firmly established (al-rasikh) which is a principle for the actions of the limbs with ease is not called character, like the disposition of writing; and by 'actions of the soul' is meant what is not specific to a limb entering into it, and by 'actions of the limbs' the opposite. His saying: (in a note if the final act is intended, or a pluck if the proximate act is intended, and it is what is mentioned in the well-known books. His saying: (if it is not a disposition) like the anger of the forbearing. His saying: (and is not a principle for the action issuing from the soul) but rather from the limbs, like the disposition of writing. His saying: (and if it is etc.) like the miser when he is generous, the generous person if he spends, his actions are inspired according to the requirement of the rational force... to submit that he enslaves the hawa' (desire) and uses it for pleasures, and the [؟] of falling into excess of pleasures properly, and the stagnation of cessation from seeking what reason and law permit from the water of pleasures. His saying: (without thinking about a note, may God have mercy on him) the well-known in the books is 'in a pluck, a pluck' because it is the action issued, and the note is only obtained from it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a definition of 'al-khuluq' (character) as a psychic disposition from which actions issue without deliberation. The matn begins by stating the objective: character is a disposition from which actions issue. It then provides examples: writing without deliberating over each letter, or playing a musical instrument without thinking about each note. The passage specifies three conditions for a psychic quality to be called 'khuluq': (1) it must be a settled disposition (malaka), not a transient state; (2) it must be a principle from which the action issues from the soul itself, not from the limbs or external faculties; and (3) the action must be performed easily and without reflection or difficulty. The commentary (sharh) elaborates on each condition: it explains that 'without deliberation' means without conscious thought, citing the dictionary definition of 'ru'yā'. It clarifies that the examples are analogies (tanzīr) not literal examples, as writing skill is a bodily disposition, not a character trait. It distinguishes between actions of the soul (which are not specific to a limb) and actions of the limbs (like writing). It provides further illustrations: transient anger in a forbearing person is not a disposition; generosity contrary to one's nature is not easy and thus not character. The passage also discusses the relation of 'quwwa' (force) in the sense of non-passivity as a dispositional quality, connecting it to the previous lesson. Overall, the lesson carefully delimits the concept of character to exclude bodily skills, transient states, and actions performed with effort.

Key Terms
