الملكة
الروية
النفس
الكيفية النفسانية
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any habitual skill or learned ability (e.g., writing, playing an instrument) is considered part of one's character (khuluq).
Correction: The passage explicitly excludes bodily skills because they are principles of action from the limbs, not from the soul itself. Character must originate from the soul and produce actions spontaneously and easily.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for something to be called 'khuluq' (character)?
A) It must be a settled quality (malakah).
B) It must be a principle of action from the soul.
C) The action must be easy and without reflection.
D) It must be a bodily faculty.

Key Takeaway
Khuluq is a stable psychic disposition from the soul that produces actions effortlessly, without deliberation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What action do you perform without thinking that reveals the true state of your soul? Are you content with what it reveals, or does it call for inner change?
?
Think of a virtuous act you performed with great effort. According to this lesson, that act does not yet reflect your character. What would it take for that act to become effortless, flowing from a settled disposition?
?
Your spontaneous reactions to hardship or praise—what do they show about your khuluq? Are you willing to examine the gap between your claimed values and your instinctive responses, and to seek transformation?

Defining Khuluq as a Firm Disposition for Action
Sanusi precisely defines khuluq with conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of khuluq as a firm psychic disposition from which actions emanate from the soul without deliberation, and the three conditions that must be met for a quality to be called character.

Core Idea
Khuluq is a psychic disposition that produces action without thought.

The fourteenth topic in this section defines a foundational term: khuluq (character). Before we can discuss moral psychology, we need a precise definition. This passage establishes that not every psychic quality counts as character—it must be a firm disposition that generates action spontaneously from the soul. The author provides clear conditions and vivid examples to distinguish true character from mere temperament or habit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own wording sink in before you start analyzing.
Notice the examples: writing without deliberating letters, playing an instrument without thinking about notes.
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage lists three restrictions that must all be present for a quality to be called khuluq. Find each one and understand why each is necessary.
The conditions are: (1) it must be a malaka (firm disposition), (2) it must be a principle for action emanating from the soul, (3) the action must occur without difficulty or reflection.
3
Reflect on the counterexamples
The passage explicitly tells us what is NOT character: a transient state, a firm disposition that does not produce action, and a disposition that produces action only with effort. Ask yourself why these are excluded.
This helps clarify the precise meaning and avoids common misconceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النسخ المشهورة الرابع عشر الخلق ملكة تصدر عنها أي عن النفس بسببها الأفعال بلا روية كمن يكتب شيئا من غير أن يروي في حرف حرف، أو يضرب الطنبور من غير أن يفكر في نغمة نغمة أو في نقرة نقرة. فالكيفية النفسانية إذا لم تكن ملكة لا تسمى خلقاً، وإذا كانت ملكة ولم تكن مبدأ لصدور الفعل عن النفس لم تسم أيضاً خلقاً، وإذا كانت مبداً له بعسر وتأمل لم تكن خلقاً، وإذا اجتمعت فيها هذه القيود معاً كانت خلقاً.
And in the well-known copies, the fourteenth [objective]: Character is a faculty from which actions emanate, meaning from the soul due to it, without deliberation, like one who writes something without deliberating over each letter, or plays the tambour without thinking about each note or each pluck. So the psychic quality, if it is not a firm disposition, is not called character. And if it is a firm disposition but is not a principle for the emanation of the action from the soul, it is also not called character. And if it is a principle for it with difficulty and reflection, it is not character. And when these restrictions combine in it together, it is character.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'khuluq' (character) as a specific type of psychic quality. The author begins by stating that character is a faculty (malaka) from which actions emanate without deliberation (bila rawiyya). He provides examples: someone writing without deliberating over each letter, or playing a musical instrument without thinking about each note. Then he specifies the conditions under which a psychic quality qualifies as character. First, it must be a firm disposition (malaka), not a transient state; otherwise, it is not called character. Second, even if it is a firm disposition, it must be a principle for the emanation of action from the soul itself, not merely from the bodily organs. Third, the action must emanate with ease, not with difficulty or reflection. When all three conditions are met, the quality is indeed character. The author thus distinguishes character from mere temperament or habit.

Key Terms
الخلق
الملكة
الكيفية النفسانية
بلا روية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any psychic quality constitutes khuluq, regardless of its stability or the ease of the action.
Correction: The author specifies that a psychic quality must be a firm disposition (malaka), a principle for action from the soul, and produce action without difficulty or reflection. Transient moods, non-motivational traits, and skills requiring effort are excluded.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for a psychic quality to be called 'khuluq'?
A) It must be a firm disposition (malaka).
B) It must be a principle for the emanation of action from the soul.
C) The action must emanate with deliberation and reflection.
D) It must produce action without difficulty.

Key Takeaway
Khuluq (character) is a psychic disposition that is firm, causes action from the soul, and produces that action spontaneously without thought or difficulty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What action do you do 'without thinking' that reveals a deep flaw in your character? Will you confront it today?
?
Think of a good deed you still do with difficulty and reluctance. What would it take for it to become your spontaneous character? What are you waiting for?
?
The lesson says if a quality does not produce action, it is not khuluq. What claim to good character do you have that never leads to action? Will you repent of that hypocrisy now?

Classification of Moral Character and the Three Powers of the Soul
The author classifies character into virtue and vice and introduces the three powers of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how moral character is classified into virtue, vice, and what is neither, and how these relate to the three powers of the rational soul and their states of mean and extremes.

Core Idea
Virtue is the mean of each soul-power's states, while vice is the extremes.

This passage begins by defining virtue and vice as principles of perfection and deficiency, respectively. Then it introduces the three powers of the rational soul needed for managing the body: intellectual, appetitive, and irascible. Finally, it connects these powers to moral character by identifying virtue with the balanced mean of each power's states and vice with the extremes. This lesson unpacks that structure and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first defines virtue and vice in general, then introduces the three powers and their roles.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Identify the three powers
Find the names of the three powers: intellectual (عقلية), appetitive (شهوية), and irascible (غضبية). Understand what each does according to the text.
Each power has a specific function: discernment, attraction, repulsion.
3
Understand the mean and extremes
Learn that each power has three possible states: two extremes (excess and deficiency) and a mean. The passage defines virtue as the mean and vice as the extremes.
There are three virtues (the means of the three powers) and six vices (two extremes per power).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينقسم الخلق إلى فضيلة هي مبدأ لما كمال هو مبدأ لما ورذيلة هي ما يكون مبدأ لما ليس شيئاً هو نقصان وغيرهما وهو منهما، والنفس الناطقة من حيث تعلقها بالبدن وتدبيرها إياه تحتاج إلى قوى ثلاث، إحداها القوة التي تعقل بها بما تحتاج إليه في تدبيره وتسمى قوة عقلية ملكية، وثانيتها القوة التي بها تجذب بها ما ينفع البدن ويلائمه وتسمى قوة شهوية بهيمية، وثالثتها ما يدفع به ما يضر البدن ويؤلمه وتسمى قوة غضبية سبعية، ولكل واحدة هذه القوى أحوال ثلاثة طرفان ووسط فالفضيلة الخلقية هي الوسط من أحوال هذه القوى والرذيلة هي الأطراف من تلك الأحوال وغيرهما أي غير الفضيلة والرذيلة ما ليس شيئا منهما أي من الوسط والأطراف، فالفضائل الخلقية أصولها ثلاثة هي الأوساط من أحوال القوى المذكورة، والرذائل الخلقية أصولها ستة هي أطرف تلك الأوساط ثلاثة منها من قبيل الإفراط وثلاثة أخرى من قبيل التفريط كلا طرفي كل الأمور ذميم
And character is divided into virtue, which is a principle for what is perfection, and vice, which is a principle for what is deficiency, and other than them, which is something from both. And the rational soul, in terms of its attachment to the body and its management of it, is need of three powers: one of them is the power by which it discerns what it needs in its management, called the intellectual, rational power; the second is the power by which it draws what benefits the body and suits it, called the appetitive, bestial power; and the third is what repels what harms the body and pains it, called the irascible, predatory power. And for each of these powers there are three states: two extremes and a mean. So the moral virtue is the mean from the states of these powers, and the vice is the extremes from those states. And other than them, i.e., other than virtue and vice, is what is not anything from them, i.e., from the mean and the extremes. So the moral virtues have three roots, which are the means from the states of the mentioned powers, and the moral vices have six roots, which are the extremes of those means: three of them are from the category of excess and three others from the category of deficiency. Both ends of every matter are blameworthy.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage classifies moral character (khuluq) into three categories: virtue (fadila), vice (radhila), and what is neither. Virtue is that which leads to perfection, vice leads to deficiency. Then the author introduces the rational soul's three powers required for managing the body: the intellectual power (quwwa 'aqliyya) for discernment, the appetitive power (quwwa shahwiyya) for drawing benefit, and the irascible power (quwwa ghadabiyya) for repelling harm. Each power has three states: two extremes (ifrat and tafrit) and a mean (wasat). Virtue is identified with the mean of each power's states, while vice is identified with the extremes. The author further explains that there are three roots of virtue (the means of the three powers) and six roots of vice (the two extremes of each power). The passage concludes with the general principle that both extremes of any matter are blameworthy.

Key Terms
الفضيلة
الرذيلة
القوة العقلية
القوة الشهوية
القوة الغضبية
الوسط
الإفراط
التفريط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that virtue is simply moderation in general, without specifying which power of the soul is being moderated.
Correction: The passage grounds virtue in specific powers of the soul: intellectual, appetitive, and irascible. Virtue is the mean state of each of these powers, not an abstract moderation.

Checkpoint
How many roots (usul) of moral vices are mentioned?
A) Three
B) Six
C) Two
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Moral virtue is the balanced mean of the soul's intellectual, appetitive, and irascible powers, while vice is any extreme.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that vice is a principle for deficiency. Think of a specific trait in your character that you know leads to spiritual deficiency—perhaps excessive anger or lack of ambition for good. How has this trait hindered your worship or relationships? What is the balanced mean you must aim for, and what concrete action will you take today to move toward that mean?
?
Your soul has three powers: intellectual, appetitive, and irascible. Do you let your appetites or anger overrule your reason? Recall a recent situation where this happened. How can you strengthen your intellectual power tomorrow so it governs your desires and emotions?
?
Both ends of every matter are blameworthy. Identify one area of your life where you tend to an extreme—either doing too much or too little. How does realizing that the mean is virtue challenge you to change? Write down one specific commitment to balance that area this week, and hold yourself accountable.
